LITTLE NUGGET.
A Record of Old Forest Creek.
BY VINOENT PYRE,
Author of “ Wild Will Enderby, "&C., &c.
(For Thu Bulletin.)
1851.
W \ ’ m LOVELIER valley than
11 jjmtkra Forest Creek, as I first
saw it in the early spring
of 1851, was not to be
found in all the broad expanse of Australia. The
summer heat had not yet scorched the tender
green of its grassy dells and soft reaches, and
the stream brightly sparkled in the sunlight as it
wound in and out and around the gently sloping
hills. Valiev and hills alike were decked with
noble gums —red and white, interspersed with
occasional box and peppermint trees, and stately
ironbarks reared their lofty columns in the higher
ridges; in sequestered nooks silver watth 9
scattered their fragrance on the warm air, and
the more rare golden acacias, often adorned with
the beautiful purple blossoms of the native sarsa-
parilla climbing lovingly around their slender
stems, delighted the twin senses of sight and
smell.
The ruthless axes of the inrushing gold-seekers
quickly denuded the forest, and the upturning of
the soil, in their eager search for its hidden
treasures, desecrated Nature’s verdant carpet and
polluted the stream ; but some lingering vestiges
of its pristine beauty remained for many years.
We were all “new-chums,” though we carefully
concealed the fact, dreading the term of blank!ty
dash “ jimmygrants ” applied to new arrivals by
“the fifty thousand vagabonds assembled round the
Mount ” (Alexander, be it understood). If a man
had not burgled his mother-in-law, or married
two sisters, stolen a horse, broken the game laws,
or perpetrated some equally atrocious deed he
“didn’t count for much,” as a gentleman from
“the other side” (a polite euphemism for Van
Dieman’s Land) once observed to me.
Yes ; we belonged to the despicable class of new-
chums ; but we flaunted it as bravely as the oldest
hand in “brab”hats and “Sydney streamers,”
serge shirts, and moleskin continuations. We
called to mind the stories that had delighted us in
boyhood, and felt that in a sort of way we were
adventurous heroes —across between Captain Kidd
and Sinbad the Sailor; and truly there was a
charm and an excitement in that wild life that I
fairly revelled in, and would fain experience over
again.
Tom, Dick, and Harry—I don’t care to particu-
larise them —were my shipmates in the old
Candahar, Captain Goss commander—144 days
out from London, and our companionship at sea
resulted in comradeship on land. Tom is a grey-
headed lawyer of good repute in Melbourne now,
and Harry is an opulent station-owner in Northern
Queensland, but Dick—cheerful, merry-hearted
Dick —was crushed to death in a “ drive ” at the
Turon. And then it came out that he was the
heir-presumptive to an English peerage—the
descendant of a very famous English admiral, who
upheld the honour of the British flag in the old
Dutch wars. After Dick’s death a Sydney
Israelite, in whom there was no guile, went up to
Government House, and produced the family
medals presented by a grateful nation to the naval
hero, and Sir Charles Fitzroy (so it was reported)
redeemed them and sent them back to the family
in England. There were plenty of such men—
men of “ good family” —knocking about in those
days, when gold raining had somewhat the aspect
of a prolonged picnic, and the adventurous life
had a charm unknown to the plodding miners of
these degenerate times.
None of us had any experience in the wielding
of picks and shovels, nor in tent-fixing and other
useful accomplishments, and we little dreamt of
gold-mining when we left old England, and tried
not to feel miserable when the Lizard light went
out of sight, and did feel as miserable as anyone
could wish when we got into the chopping waters
of the Bay of Biscay. But whilst we were crawling
over the sea, the “ root of all evil ” —which is just
the seed of all good—had been discovered and the
news of Forest Creek had reached Melbourne when
we anchored in Hobson’s Bay,
After sundry migrations we pitched our tent in
a little glade—a mere dimple in the ranges — which
had several attractions for us. In the first place,
it was apart from the diggings—overlooking them
from a slight rock-bound elevation, at the fo t of
which oozed a tiny spring of cool, pure water—an
important; consideration now that a summer sun
was blazing and water was selling at half-a-crown
a bucket. Another attraction was the seclusion
of our chosen camping-ground. There was only
one other tent in the gully, and the tent-keeper
was a neat, motherly woman (women of any sort
were scarce on the diggings), whose appearance
satisfied us of the respectability of the surround-
ings. We discovered afterwards that her boarders
wereVandemonians of the deepest dye, and that she
was another. But they were very good neighbours
for all that; and indeed I always found that the
safest “ pitch ” was in the immediate vicinity of
this class. They had an unwritten code of some
sort, one of the articles of which, scrupulously ob-
served, was—“Thou shalt not rob thy neighbour,
but only him who is at a distance from thee,” and
1 never knew this commandment to be broken.
The range behind us—densely timbered —had a
low saddle, which led into Donkey Gully, where
there were some considerable workings, but it was
not often used, as it was out of the ordinary route.
But one fine morning an incident occurred to
break the monotony of our We had
been firing off our pistols ere reloading them;
also to let all whom it concerned understand that
we had arms and ammunition at command. The
general—I might say the universal —practice en
the diggings was to fire off pistols and guns at
sundown, and what with the discharge of firearms,
the barking of dogs, and hoarse shouting of men,
there was every evening as pretty a how-d’ye-do
as could be desired But, being of the despised
new-chum species, We regarded the business of
firing off as being more appropriate and impressive
in the morning, when the stillness was unbroken
save by the rocking of multitudinous cradles,
which sent up a commingled roar as of many fac-
tories at work. Moreover, we wanted to be expert
shots, and, therefore, we blazed away at a barked
target on a gum-tree about thirty yards away up
the gully. That tree was not much injured by
our experimental warfare ; but, then, our weapons
were “pepper-boxers”—so-called—revolvers with
six barrels, and in my own case a pair of toy*
pistols, carrying ballets a little larger than swan-
shot, and only dangerous to the user. Well, we
had wastefully expended our ammunition as usual
on that particular morning, and seated ourselves
contentedly on a log in front of the fire to enjoy
the flavour and fragrance of the morning pipe,
when down the gully came a dainty youngster
with “ new chum ” written all over him in
unmistakeable character’s. He was a dapper
youth, apparently about eighteen years of age
—certainly not more, short in stature and of
slender figure, and he wore his hair in long curly
locks—a fashion much affected at that time—
which gave him rather an effeminate appearance.
We stared at seeing anyone coming from that
direction. Such an event had never happened
before.
“Good morning, gentlemen,” said the stranger;
and he spoke in a soft, mellow, voice that in some
way touched a chord of sympathy. Evidently he
was a very “ new chum,” for that was not exactly
the sort of language in vogue amongst the habitues
of Forest Creek.
We returned his polite salutation, and then said
he— “I beg your pardon; but have you been
firing up the gully ? ”
We admitted the soft impeachment.
“Well,” quoth our visitor, with a pleasant
smile and a rather sarcastic twinkle of his
eyes, “you were nearly the death of me—
look at this.” And he showed us a bullet-
“ I have been hunting, too, and here is the game I have bagged .”
hole in the loose folds of his serge shirt, in peril-
ous proximity to the region of the heart.
It is unnecessary to tell how profusely we
apologised. The end of it was that the stranger
sat down on our log, and drank a pannikin of tea
with us. Smoke he would not. He said tobacco
disagreed with him. Bit by bit, he told us how
he was working all by himself just over the range,
and not having struck anything good he had
strolled over our way, on the look-out for some-
thing better. We were so well pleased with his
appearance and pleasant manner that we forth-
with proposed he should “chum in” with our
selves. But no ; he preferred to be alone, only, if
we had no objection, he would like to pitch his
tent near ours. And when we came back for the
midday meal he was busy fixing a picanniny tent,
with a luxurious stretcher and —above all things
—a camp-stool for furniture.
From that time forth he was our companion.
But he always wrought by himself—going away
in the morning with a tin-dish and an attenuated
pick and shovel that looked more like playthings
than implements of labour, yet he managed to
gather a fair average quantity of gold in his own
fashion. He seemed very pleased when we asked
him to join our mess. Indeed after one timid
offer to attend to the flesh-pots—the camp-oven
and frying-pan—he succeeded so well that we
unanimously elected him chief cook and bottle-
washer of the establishment, and remitted his
share of the expenses in consideration of his
culinary services. Such unexpected dainties as he
used to turn out for our delectation— such savoury
pancakes and tender “ dough-boys ” and luxurious
“ plum-duff ! ” Nothing very epicurean, it is true,
but the health-inspiring vocation of the miner and
the bushman makes delicacies of many things that
are “common to the general.”
Very soon Joe —that was the only name he gave
—became quite a pet with us. Only Dick would
persist in calling him “ Missy,” on account of his
effeminate appearance, and Joe did not like this
at all. I remember one evening when Dick had
been more than usually aggressive in this way,
Joe —flashing an angry glance at' him from his
dark blue evee—ociea out: “ Why do yew inwilt
me ? Am I not as good a man as any ei you 1 I
will shift my tent in the morning.”
And then we fell upon Dick, and made his life
very uncomfortable until he apologised to Joe,
and promised, like a good little boy, never to do it
any more. After that Dick was mighty civil to
our pet, and always wanted to be doing this thing
and the other for him. But Joe would have none
of it. Harry—the surly old bear, as we regarded
him, who was about as taciturn a member of the
human brotherhood as I ever chanced to meet —
was Joe’s favourite. Harry never “fussed.” He
would fetch logs for the fire, and water for the
“ billy,” and all without speaking a word, except
when Joe would thank him. That seemed to
irritate him. “What’s the use of bothering?”—
he would growl. “Ain’t I better able to do it
than a boy like you?” And then Joe, never
taking offence, would laugh and sing a snatch of
the milkmaid’s song—“ Nobody axed you, sir, she
said.”
Altogether Joe was a puzzler. After many
serious consultations over our pip s, we came to
the conclusion that he came of good stock, and
had run away from home in some boyish freak.
We were not far astray in this conjecture, as it
afterwards turned out. Remember, the eldest of
us was actually twenty-four years of age, and we
felt and reasoned like the “grave and reverend
seigniors ”we believed ourselves to be. lam sure
that one at least of us felt more sedate and
Solomon-like in those far-off days than now.
One of the youngster’s fancies was the accumu-
lation of nuggets. He had a positive passion for
the shining lumps of gold. One day I chanced
upon a nugget of about two ounces weight. It
was shaped somewhat like a pear with a small
hole at the thin end, and freckled with specks of
milk-white quartz. Joe’s eyes-always as bright
as a stag’s—actually danced with delight when I
exhibited it. He ran away to his tent, and bring-
ing the match-box—the little wooden cylinder
then in use—in which he kept his stores, he
begged me to give it to him in exchange for an
equal weight of gold. Now, I wanted to keep it
for a certain young person ; but somehow Joe
always had his own way, and he coaxed me into
making the transfer. He got a fresh nickname
through this affair. Mischievous Dick bestowed
it on him, and he was as often called “ Little
Nugget ” as by his own name thenceforth.
One day Joe disappeared. When morning
dawned the lithe young figure was missing from
the camp-fire. “ The young rascal is asleep,”
said Tom, and, “I’ll soon rouse the little beggar,”
cried Dick. But Harry, saying never a word,
strode over to Joe’s tent; after shouting in vain
for the owner to “Come out of that,” he untied
the fastenings of the entrance, and tumbled in,
only to tumble out again, muttering a savage im-
precation. “By Jove! he has bolted,” said he,
“ blankets and all.” And so he had, and we saw
nothing of him that day, nor the next, nor for
many days thereafter.
Now, it was a curious thing, but it so happened
that we became very cross and cantankerous after
Joe’s disappearance. Something seemed to have
gone out of our lives, and we bickered with each
other—quarrelling would be too strong a phrase—
without provocation—aye, and over our pipes,
too. Joe had so thoroughly wound his way into
our hearts that each of us felt his absence as a
personal affliction, and we roundly abused one
another for having been the cause of his depar-
ture. Mutual recrimination on this score was
ceaseless, but neither of us would own to having
given the youngster any offence. I had a sneak-
ing suspicion which I prudently kept to myself,
because Dick was a much more muscular Chris-
tian than I, that he had been making too free
with his jibes and jeers for Joe’s patience. Cer-
tainly the absconder was but a poor sort of
“molly-coddle” in my superior estimation ; but
I loved the lad with the patronising kind of affec-
tion that one bestows on pets—human and other-
wise.
However, one evening, just as the sun was set-
ting behind piled-up banks of purple clouds,
barred and flecked with stripes and patches of
crimson and gold, Master Joe came daintily trip-
ping down the gully, and bade us “ good-day !” as
placidly as if he had just stepped out from his
tent hard by.
“ Good day, mates !” said he, as cool as a cucum-
ber, slipping his “ swag ” easily to the ground;
“ have you any tea in the billy ?”
And with a swift deft motion, all his own, he
took a pannikin, and helped himself without more
ado.
“ Wall, I’m blessed exclaimed Harry ; and al
of us cried, “ Well, I’n. ,” filling in the hiatus
in divers ways, according to our various moods.
“ Where have you been, you daft young gipsy?’*
queried Harry. “Do you know we have been
scouring the country for you, and should have set
the “traps” on your track only they’re so busy
digger-hunting that they can’t attend to anything
less important—yourself, for instance?”
The young fellow absolutely blushed at this
rude speech; but, quickly recovering himself, he
said : “1 have been hunting, too, and here is the
game I have bagged. ”
And he field up for our inspection a nugget of
about 12 ounces, as we gauged it with our experi-
enced eyes. A fine lump it was—solid and un-
mixed with quartz. Shall I confess it? I felt
envious of Joe’s luck. Here were we, pegging
away in a beastly hole some fourteen feet deep, to
work in which was reckoned a reckless risk of
death and danger in those days, when six, or at
the most, eight feet was as far as the ordinary
digger cared to venture into “the bowels of the
land.” And all we had gotten for our pains, after
six weeks’ toil and trouble —our sore hand-blister-
ing and back-aching exertions —was less than a
half of Joe’s handsome nugget.
“Look here, now,” quoth Dick, “you had
better give that to the preacher chap next Sun-
day.”
Now, “ the preacher chap,” as Dick irreverently
called him, was an elderly man of unctuous
speech and oily appearance from the crown of his
hat to the soles of his boots, with special additions
of grease about the collar and cuffs of his rusty
black coat, and the bulging knees of his still
rustier trousers. He used to mount a stump
whenever he could collect an audience and deliver
a sort of religious discourse, liberally sprinkled
with invocations to the Deity, and promises of hot
weather in the next world, interspersed with
surely the most doleful of all possibly doleful
hymns. Whether he was a sincere enthusiast or
not I cannot tell, but as the principal part of these
al fresco services “lay in the application thereof,”
which consisted in the business known as “ send-
ing round the hat,” it seemed to me that the
worship of Mammon rather unduly'predominated.
None of us believed in him. Tom denounced him
as “a fraud,” Dick said he was “a precious old
humbug,” Harry (whose father was a rural dean
at home) called him “an impostor.” As for Joe,
he positively detested the man. Dick’s suggestion,
therefore, was not well received by our young
friend. “No,” he said, “if I give it to anyone
someone I love shall have it.”
The expression did not strike us as curious at
the time, but it did afterwards.
“Don’t keep it about the tent, Joe,” said I;
“ sell it or take it to the bank, or you will have
such little brains as you possess rudely scattered
if it should be suspected that there is such a prize
in your possession.”
(Par parenthlse, I may say that the Bank of
New South Wales then flew its blue flag over a
tent in “the Camp” at “ the Junction ” as the
confluence of Forest and Barker’s Creek was
■ designated.)
“Thank you. Jack ” —I was Jack—“thank you
for the compliment. It is very nice of you to say
that, but I don’t care to part with my pretty
nuggets for fifty shillings an ounce ” —the then
current price on the creek. And with that he
broke out in a peal of merry, boyish laughter,
shaking his sunny curls from his forehead ; and I
pictured to myself some dear home in the far-off
mother-land of which he had perhaps been the
pride. Boor lad ! he was so very young in all his
ways to be alone, save for us, his chance friends,
in the great wilderness of the South.
Our happy days in Centipede Gully (that was
the name we gave it, for hundreds of excellent
reasons) came to an end all too soon. Of course
the primal cause was woman. One fine morning
when we went down to our spring, we found to
our dismay that two intruding fiends, in the guise
of washerwomen, had cet up a laundry there and
defiled the only drinkable water in the neighbour-
hood. To this day I owe those women a grudge,
for it caused the breaking-up of our pleasant
party. We had to use other and impure
water —muddy, and charged with mineral matter.
The result was that we all suffered, more or less,
from illness, and Dick worst of all. So he
set out in search of a doctor. There were
plenty around, but most of them were more pro-
fitably engaged in sluicing gold than in drenching
patients. However, Dick found a tent on Bed
Hill, on which appeared a written calico sign—
“ Jeremiah Rogers, Docter ” (so it was spelled).
On inquiring for the “Docter ” he was introduced
to a burly fellow in a red serge shirt, with sleeves
tucked up to the elbows, and a dripping sheath-
knife in his mouth, busily engaged in “dressing”
a sheep. Putting a grimy hand on Dick’s pulse,
pounding him in the ribs, making him show his
tongue, and performing other antics peculiar to
the profession, “Docter” Rogers first demanded
a sovereign and then prescribed brandy.
Now, at that time, total prohibition was the law
on the gold-fields. Licenses for the sale of liquor
were not allowed to be issued for any house
situated within five miles of the gold-fields’
boundary. As a natural result, grog - shanties
flourished in great numbers. None of us had ever
entered these dens ; so where to get brandy Dick
did not know. He explained the difficulty to the
“ docter.”
“Oh. well, said that eminent practitioner, wip-
ing his knife on his moleskin continuations, “I
keep a small quantity for medicinal purposes, and
as you are really very bad I’ll let you have a bottle
for two pounds.”
Dick paid the price demanded by the shanty-
keeping doctor, and brought it to the camp.
There he warmed some water, put in some of the
drugged medicinal brandy, added sugar also, and
drank the villainous stuff with much apparent
relief and satisfaction. He beamed so brightly
over it, and the fumes from his pannikin were so
pleasant that we all wanted some of his medicine.
More than that, by unanimous consent we took,
and partook of it, until we became first cheerful,
then hilarious, then controversial, then angry,
and finally wound up with a regular breakmg-up
quarrel.
In this unhappy business Joe had no part. He
made a grimace when the steaming poison was
passed round to him, and, putting it aside, fled to
the refuge of his own tent. “It isn’t good,
Harry,” he cried—Harry was always his favourite
—“ It isn’t good. Don’t drink it! ”
“ I can’t make that youngster out at all,” said
Dick. “He doesn't smoke, and he won’t drink,
and he is as squeamish as a school-miss.”
“No,” growled Harry, “I don’t suppose you
can make him out. You see he has been brought
up, and you were only dragged up.”
Then Dick, with the blood of his fighting
ancestry surging in his veins, knocked Harry back
over the log we were sitting on, sending the scald-
ing liquor into his face. A rough-and-tumble
fight ensued, in which we all got mixed up some-
how. Everybody hammered away at the most
proximate body without discrimination, quite
content to hit somebody. Suddenly Joe rushed
fn amcmgwb us and dragged Harry out erf the


tangle without a word, and we saw them no more
that night. In truth we were all very thankful for
this seasonable interruption of our too-animated
discussion, and we adjourned to seek repose in
We met next morning all sullen, downcast, and
ashamed. With few words we divided our stock
0 { gold and dissolved partnership. Tom wended
his way to Melbourne and went in for the practice
of his profession like a sensible man. Dick
migrated to the Turon. Said Harry to me : “ What
are you going to do ? ” 1 told him I should follow
suit, and so I did, for Dick and I went round to
Sydney together. .
I sadly wanted Joe to come with us. .1 was
vexed and somewhat dispirited, thinking of the
bright little lad remaining, all alone, amongst the
rough crowd of diggers. “Come with us, Little
Nugget,” I urged, “ we will take care of you, be
Bl Joe only tossed his saucy head, and laughed
with a musical, rippling laugh, that somehow
made me more sad than tears would have done.
« Don’t you think I can take care of myself ?” he
asked. “ Only do one thing before you go. See
me safe to the camp with my gold, and when you
are gone, I’ll go and gather more nuggets. I mean
to have quite a number, I assure you.”
We volunteered to go in a body as an escort with
Little Nugget, but he said : “Oh ! one is enough.
You go with me, Harry.”
And so it was arranged, and the rest of us
shouldered our swags and started for Melbourne,
Harry promising to overtake us if we went slowly.
We did go slowly—very slowly—but Harry never
overtook us. And I thought I had lost sight of
Joe and Harry for ever.
1852.
After a brief spell Dick and I started for the
Sydney goldfields, where we worked at Ophir and
the Turon. But luck was against us, and I wended
my way back to Melbourne while Dick found his
fate in the way Ihave narrated. Just as Igotthere
the first escort from Eaglehawk came in with nearly
seven tons of gold, guarded by a corps of pen-
sioners, or “military settlers,” as they were
termed, who had been brought over from Van
Dieman’s Land to do duty in the absence of the
regular police. I witnessed their arrival at the old
Treasury in William-street, after their many days
patient plodding through the bush. Hardy old
veterans they were, some with only one arm, and
others with wooden legs, but the boldest bushranger
would have reckoned the cost twice be f ore ven-
turing to encounter them. This sight induced me
to try my fortune at Bendigo, first and properly
known as “ Bandicoot ” Creek. But I had a diffi-
culty in finding mates, for I was rather particular
as to whom I foregathered with, having a well-
grounded objection to consorting with strangers
of dubious antecedents, who might find it con-
venient or profitable to sever my carotid artery,
or drop me into a waterhole. Some days, there-
fore, passed and found me still mooning about
Melbourne. I sought out Tom, but he was
immersed in Chitty and Blackstone, and would
not listen. “ I’ve had enough of digging,” he
averred; “gold is all very well in the concrete,”
said he, “ but I prefer it in the abstract.”
“That is contrary to the old proposition,”
quoth I. ...
“Ah! yes; but if my clients get it in the
concrete I can more easily abstract it.” And
with that he returned to his musty parchments
and precedents.
But the very next morning, as luck would have
it, whilst I was breakfasting at the old Royal
Hotel in Collins-street (afterwards the Criterion,
and now a bank) a hand was laid on my shoulder
pot lightly—l was almost surprised into choking
by the concussion—and a well-known voice saluted
me _“ Well, Jack, old boy ! how goes it?”
I jumped up in impulsive ecstacy. The voice
was the voice of Harry, and the hand was his
also.
We were so glad to meet that we made a long
day of it. We called in the solitary waiter, who
was nearly all that remained of the establishment,
and commenced proceedings by ordering in not
champagne, for champagne was a, vulgar liquor in
those days, but beer —real, British bottled beer,
charged for at the rate of about a crown a bottle,
from each of which, with the infectious recklessness
of the period, we only poured one glass, disdaining
the remainder. When we were tired of that we
went about the town and developed other eccen-
tricities; and in the evening we patronised the
dingy hole called a theatre, in Queen-street, ad-
joining the St. John’s Tavern, kept by the re-
doubtable John Thomas, who afterwards bloomed
into a perennial Mayor of Melbourne. And in
that pandemonium we saw the Young family gesti-
culating on the stage, amidst an indescribable up-
roar that prevented anything being heard, and
narrowly escaping annihilation from the empty
bottles and other missiles playfully tossed about
by the occupants of the dress-circle. They threw
gold about, too, but that was reserved for those
performers who danced or ranted to their satis-
faction. Otherwise, when they failed to come up
to the mark, bottles made them understand that
better things were expected of them.
Next day we had cogent private reasons for not
being very merry. However, in the afternoon we
did to business seriously incline, and made a com-
pact fast and sure to go once more together on the
gold path.
“And what about Joe —our Little Nugget? ”
“Ah ! ” said Harry ; “ that’s a mystery. Do
you know I was very fond of that little beggar,
and, by Jove ! —yes —well, I lost him.”
“Lost him! Lost Joe!” I cried. “Please
explain.”
r< Well,’ said Harry, “ I believe that nugget of
yours was the primary cause of the mischief. ou
remember I went with him to lodge his gold in
the bank. He would not sell it outright, but got
a small advance on it, and he kept back the nugget
that he wheedled you out of.
( “ When he came out of the bank he said :
' Harry, I have not had any letter since I came
to the Creek, and I am sure there are some waiting
tor me somewhere. ’
“ I asked him if he had given his address at the
Melbourne Post-office, and ho told me he had.
‘Did you tell Captain King to inquire for you at
Kyneton ?’ I asked again. You know ‘ Captain ’
King, as he called himself, used to fetch any letter
he was authorised to get for people at the Kyne-
ton office, 20 miles off—the usual depot for Mount
Alexander, charging a shilling a letter for Ins
trouble. ‘ Yes, he had done that.’ Well, then,
I told him the only other chance was that they
flight have been sent ' o the Porcupine, nine miles
on the other side of Forest Creek, on the Bendigo
Hoad. ‘Oh ! Harry,’ says Joe, 4 will you go over
there with me to inquire V And I did. and that’s
wl ;y I never overtook you and Dick.
We started over to the Porcupine next day,
which was Sunday, but there were no letters for
Joe, and the poor little beggar fairly broke down
Mid cried with vexation, saying he supposed_ no-
body cared for him, and he was without a single
tPlfend, and all that sort of thing, you know. I
rmd to chee'r him up a bit. and got him to eat
something—drink he wouldn’t —and as he was
tired and footsore, I proposed to stay for the
night. But the landlord, who seemed to be a
decent sort of fellow in his way, looked grave
when I sounded him about beds. ‘Look here,’ he
says, ‘ you appear to be respectable young fellows,
and I wouldn’t advise you to stop here to-night.
You see there is only this public-house anywhere
near the diggings, and a lot of rough fellows
come over here every Sunday—not your sort at
all, I can see. Go home, boys, if you’ve got any
sort of a one, for your own sake, and that’s my
advice.’
“ And very good advice it was, too ; so away we
started on the return trip. The miners were
already beginning to congregate about the house,
and I noticed a hang-dog looking customer,
with greasy Vandemonian ringlets, strolling
after us. But I said nothing, because I did
not want to alarm Joe. However, when we got to
a big waterhole at the junction of the Mount
Alexander-road and Barker’s Creek, the youngster
complained of his sore feet, and said he must
bathe them, and (you know what a particular
fellow he was) he went round under a big gum
that grows there and took off his boots and socks
for the purpose. Presently, the rascal who had
dodged us all the way from the Porcupine came
along, and asked for a light for his pipe, and
wanted to have a friendly chat. Perhaps I was a
trifle hasty, but I did not like the look of him, and
advised him to clear out. Then he got impudent,
and—well, I just pulled out my pepper-box, you
know, and fired a shot o v er his head. “What’s
that for ?” he asked ; and I saw he was a cur by
the droop of his under-lip. So I quietly turned
the barrel of my pistol and cocked it, mildly

have sought high and low, enquired at the bank,
and all that sort of thing ; but I have never seen
or heard of Joe from that day to this.”
Four months or more had passed away when
Harry and I, after a fairly profitable sojourn at
Eaglehawk and Spring Gully, joined the big rush
to Moonlight at Forest Creek, where we chanced
on some very pretty ground. One Sunday morn-
ing we were aroused from our slumbers at day-
break by the angry roar of a turbulent mob. We
had pitched our tent on the slope of a rise, and
from this post of vantage we could see what was
gomg on. In a broad flat, aside and apart from
the main workings, a large crowd had collected,
forming a wide ring. They were yelling like
maniacs, and at first it appeared as if a pugilistic
encounter was under way. But further observa-
tion revealed something different. In the centre
of the ring stood a solitary human figure, around
whom some five or six men slowly and continu-
ously perambulated. “Get the breakfast ready,_
Jack, while I go down and see what it s all about,
said Harry. . ,
Just then some miners came away from trie
crowd, and of them, as they passed by, we en-
quired the cause of the disturbance. They told us
that the central figure had stuck up an old man
and robbed him of his gold, and that the miners
wanted to lynch the culprit, but that some sailors
the men revolving around him— had sent to the
Camp for the authorities there to take charge of
him and whilst waiting their arrival they were at
once keeping him in custody and guarding him
from violence “Lynch him !-string him up !”
cried the angry and excited crowd. But the
sailors steadily refused to give up their prisoner.
“No !” they answered, and they clinched their
denial with a “mouth-filling oath” that would
have satisfied Hotspur himself : “ No; we ain’t
going to allow any Yandemonian tricks or Yankee
fooleries to be played off on these diggings. We
are holding the young fellow, and we mean to keep
him in tow till the ‘traps’ come for him, if it
takes us all day ; and if he has robbed that old son
of a sea cook as says so, why he’ll get all he
ot a sea-GOOK as says »<J, Ll .r
deserves in a proper manner according to the
articles of war. And, by thunder, if any of you
try to take him from us, we’ll let daylight into
some of your hides.” .
This little oration had its intended effect, and
the conservators of order continued their ceaseless
perambulations around the wretched object of
their attentions. And, as we gazed, we saw him
turn his head from side to side, as if in search of a
sympathetic face.
I set the camp-oven and the billy on the fire,
and then turned to see how the trouble was pro-
grossing. Harry had disappeared in the thron n ,
and as I did not feel particularly interested, I
attended to my cooking operations with the zest
begotten of a well-set appetite, sharpened by the
keen air of an Australian spring morning.
It was a beautiful scene that I gazed upon as
placidly smoked my well-seasoned cutty. The shafts
of the uprising sun were glinting through the
branches and pendulous leaves of the stately white
gum-trees and playing around their boles, chequer-
mg the carpet of earth with ever-ranging traceries
where the rich, red soil gleamed Jrough the bright
green grass, save where upturned piles of clay and
gravel disfigured the landscape. Even the distant
reaches of the muddy creek glistened in the sun-
light. And in the face of it all that grim drama,
which might at any moment turn to tragedy, was
being enacted. As the hours passed on the crowd
became more ominously sullen, and, as I judged,
more dangerous. The prisoner had dropped on
the ground as from exhaustion, and still his
keepers marched round and round the prostrate
Jgure, revolvers in their hands, with patient,
persistent, forceful monotony. The day wore on,
and the sun had mounted nearly to the zenith,
and none of the officials from the camp had put
in an appearance. Suddenly several shots were
fired, whether at the prisoner or not I cannot say;
and the mob surged backwards, then forward, and
broke up in confusion The prisoner and his
guardians were merged in the indiscriminate mass
of human beings. The uproar became terrible,
but in the midst of it I heard a potent voice, that
I thought I recognised as Harry’s, crying out to
someone to “Run!” and a lithe figure —that of
the prisoner—suddenly burst through the crowd.
He came flying like a hunted deer, across the flat
and over the workings, leaping from hea,p to heap
of the upturned soil with desperate rapidity, and
close at his heels followed the enraged miners,
howling and shouting like infuriated demons.
Just as he passed below the point on which
I was standing, a red faced virago darted from
a tent near by, and, crossing his track dealt him
a mighty blow from her brawny arm, felling him
tothe earth. Like wolves athirst for blood, the
pursuers fastened on their victim, and half dragged,
half carried him to the side of the range. A coil of
rope was taken from a neighbouring store without
the formality of purchase—a noose quickly
knotted was placed round the prisoner’s neck, and
the other end cast over the projecting branch of a
convenient gumtree amidst cries of savage exulta-
tion. The victim, now insensible, was held in an
erect position ; half-a-dozen burly ruffians seized
the loose end of the rope in impatient expectancy
of the signal ; the sacrifice was about to be cqp-
summated. I saw Harry and some of the sailors
desperately attempting to effect a rescue, and
endeavoured to join them. But we were over-
powered by numbers, and the end seemed close at
hand. “ Hoist away !” shouted the ringleaders,
when just at that awful moment four horsemen
appeared on the range above. It was the Com-
missioner and his mounted escort, The lynchers
paused, and allowed the rope to swing loose, and
the inert body of the prisoner, released from sup-
port, fell in a tumbled heap at the foot of the tree.
Either the Commissioner was frightened by the
spectacle or he lost control of his horse, for, as
a Northumbrian observed to me, “he kept filling
and backing, like a Geordie running down chan-
nel.” At last he managed to gasp out : ‘My
good men, whatever you do don’t take the law
into your own hands.” .
Considering that Mr. Commissioner had been
six hours coming a mile to do his duty, and that,
but for the pluck of the sailors an innocent life
would have been sacrificed, this has always
seemed the most amazing piece of moral advice
that I ever heard enunciated.
Harry, breathless and bleeding, with myself
and some of the better disposed, struggled to the
front. Our first care was to cast loose the rope,
our next to raise the still insensible prisoner. One
look was enough. It was Joe—our own Little
Nugget ! „ , . ~
Her clothing had oeen torn from her shoulders
(it is useless to palter with the pronoun any
longer), and I saw that what Harry had told me
was correct. Others saw the same evidence.
Some of the wretches jeered, but more had suffi
cient humanity to shrink away abashed.
“ What—what—what is the matter?” stam-
mered Mr. Commissioner.
“He has robbed me,” shrieked Joe’s accuser
—a man with long, greasy ringlets. “He
has got my nugget on him now. I can swear
to it anywhere.” And he described, with mar-
vellous accuracy, the very nugget that I had
parted with to Joe—my own “ first find ”on
Forest Creek. Then he approached, as if to make
a search, but Harry strode between—“ Stand
back, you scoundrel!” he cried, his face blazing
with honest wrath, and I regret to say that he
made use of very strong language, utterly ignoring
the majesty of the law as represented m the per-
son of Mr. Commissioner. , , ,
“No one shall touch her but me,” he declared.
“ Don’t you see it’s a woman ?” And then, with
all delicacy and tenderness, he drew from Joe’s
pocket the veritable nugget and told its story,
which I, of course, confirmed.
Then the tide of popular feeling turned and the
hunter became the hunted. The false accuser was
set upon and violently hustled by the mob, which
panted for something or somebody upon which to
expend its aroused passions. I believe they would
have hung him with the rope prepared for Joe
but for the presence of the Commissioner. As it
was, the scoundrel was treated to such a course of
mud-baths and other indignities as satisfied their
ardour for justice and their thirst for amusement.
Harry told me afterwards that he was the fellow
who had dogged them at the Porcupine.
Poor Harry was so badly hurt in this affray
that he had to take up his quarters in the Mel-
bourne Hospital for a time. And Joe —well, Joe
had had enough of mining adventure and hence-
forth very prudently abandoned garments propria
quce maribus.
I learned Joe’s story (her name was really
Josephine) long afterwards. It was simple enough.
She had run away from an uncongenial step-
mother and donned the disguise of boy’s clothing
as a protection in the lawless state of the diggings.

1877.
A quarter of a century passed away. I had long
since migrated to New Zealand ; and the memory
of the events I have recorded had become as a,
dream of the distant Past, when I received a letter
from Harry.
Dear old Jack ! (Thus it was worded.) I hear that you
are located at the Dunstan. We have been “ doing” the
o-rand scenery of your beautiful country, and shall be
passing through Clyde next Monday. Do meet us when
the coach passes through. I want to take you by the
hand again, and, above all, to introduce you to Mrs
Harry.
Then followed this postscript
Do you still remember Centipede Gully and Moonlight?
Be sure I met the coach, and quickly recognised
Harry, though he had aged considerably in the
sultry Queensland climate. He introduced me to
a little woman, not young nor particularly hand-
some, but well-preserved and beautiful with the
beauty that comes with middle age. There were
“ silver threads amongst the gold,” but the dark-
blue eyes still sparkled brightly, and a sweet
smile illumined her face as she offered me her
hand. “ Don’t you know me, Jack?” she cried.
“ I’m Joey the' Little Nugget, you know—and
(divinely regardless of grammar) I have it yet.”
Few and brief were the words we exchanged,
fos Harry was in haste to get back to his herds
and flocks. A glass of wine, and then—“ All
aboard !” A crack of the driver’s whip, a rattle
of wheels and brakes, a prancing of horses, and
Joe and Harry passed away like
A Tale that is Told.


THE SPELL
OF THE
MAS SHANTOO
Pontianak, at the
mouth of the Kapoeas
River, is not a place
much visited by Euro-
peans, but one can
obtain an exceptional
experience there.
Pontianak is the
head-quarters of the
Dutch in Borneo, and
the Resident-General
has a small joke of
his own, which he plays
off on the unsuspecting
new chum. As is cus-
tomary in those torrid
settlements, business is
generally transacted dur-
ing £he comparatively
cool hours immed i ately
succeeding daylight. As
you discuss it with the
courteous old resident,
he inveigles you into a
stroll up and down the
verandah, and after a
slight experience of this
exercise, he informs you
that the equatorial line
passes right through the
centre of his bungalow,
and that during that
morning walk you have
crossed and re-crossed the
equator several times.
I have other cause to
remember Pontianak. It
was my starting-point
on an expedition destined
to be =. very memorable
one. L had long con-
templated a trip into
the interior of Borneo,
allured partly by the
reports of the half-worked
diamond mines, and part-
ly by natural curiosity to
see a place so little known.
I had accidentally met
with a young travelling
Englishman, an enthusi-
astic sportsman, who
eagerly jumped at the
notion, and the result
was that we soon found
ourselves at Pontianak,
where, after the necessary
official permission had
been obtained, we made
our arrangements for de-
parture.
Travelling there is a
far more luxurious style
3f thing than in the Aus-
tralian back-blocks. Our
destination was the
Sintang district, and
our highway the river
Kapoeas. A large roofed-
in native boat, known as
a gobang, a native crew
under a mandor or head-
man ; a good outfit of
stores, &0., were obtained,
and we started for the
land of the Dyaks.
For days our travel-
ling was most auspicious.
The dense jungle on
either hand afforded a
good supply of animal
life for my companion to
exercise his sporting in-
stincts on, and the native
tribes we met were
friendly and interesting.
As time went on we
found ourselves amongst
Dyaks—permission to
pass through whose terri-
tory cost some diplomacy,
but patience and a
friendly demeanour over-
came all objections, and
we soon got well into
the mountainous districts
on the upper reaches of
the river. As yet I had
not met the object of my search—the aban- |
doned diamond mines, legends of which
were often repeated by the coastal Malays.
Once or twice I was shown places where gold-
mining on a most primitive fashion had un
doubtedly been pursued in some long-forgotten
time. Circular holes had been sunk in three
places in the shape of a triangle. Drives had
then been made from one to another, but by
whom it had been done the Dyaks could not
tell. Certainty not by their forefathers. Some
told me that it was the work of slaves long
ago, when the sultans from India had swept
down on the archipelago and enthroned them-
selves in Java and Sumatra, thence enforcing
tribute over Borneo, Celebes and the smaller
islands.
No ruins nor inscriptions were to be found
indicating that the country had ever been per-
manently settled by the men of that time. Some-
times I heard mysterious reports of a wild race
whose descent was more ancient than that of the
Dyaks; they were known as the Orangpooenan,
of forest men, and were marked with a white spot
in the middle of the forehead, a proof, at any rate,
of their Hindu origin.
One day Morton and I were dozing inside our
mosquito-nets, when frantic yells of pain from the
boatmen aroused us, and next moment they all
plunged overboard. We sat looking at each other
in silent astonishment. Everything seemed
peaceful enough : what could have caused such a
dispersion ? Presently,, one after another, the
men re-appeared and climbed back into the boat,
when we learned the cause of it. A swarm of
bees had suddenly invaded the gobang during
their flight across the river, and being most
pugnacious insects had routed the crew, flying on
when the men took to the water. Morton and I
being inside mosquito-nets had escaped.
This incident happened on the last day of our
progress. That afternoon about 4 o’clock the
mandor came to me and pointed to a rope of
twisted rattan stretched across the river—a sign
that we were to go no further. Some Dyaks
were assembled on the bank, and we went ashore
to parley with them.
They were apparently as friendly as usual, and
accepted some small presents of tobacco, but
declined to give us any reason for refusing the
required permission to proceed. We visited the
village and partook of fruit there, and after dark
returned to our boat. Morton was very hurt at our
sudden detention, and wanted to go on in spite of
the natives. I pointed out to him the folly cf such
a proceeding, and he consented to take things
quietly and wait for a day or two. During those
two days I made every effort to conciliate our
neighbours, and with perfect success, excepting in
the one direction. We were not to go up the
river. I could get no reason for this refusal, and
came to the conclusion that we must perforce
return.
My enquiries as to the ancient gold and
diamond mines seemed to amuse the old men
mightily. One of them told me that I had seen
the Kambing-Mas. This is a golden sheep which
appears to certain doomed men; so infatuated
does the victim become at sight of it, that he
follows it on through jungles and mountains day
after day, until he dies of fatigue.
Of legends and traditions I got my fill, but per-
mission to proceed was not obtainable. My old
“ Behind me, as I squatted by the water's edge, the two yellow, semi-nude figures, of the old
man and the girl, bending over my shoulders in rapt attention."
friend who told me of the Kambing-Mas asked me
if I wanted to try for the great diamond which
was supposed to be in a lake at the head of a river,
This gem, described of enormous size and un-
speakable lustre, can be plainly seen at the bottom,
like a star, but woe to the rash man who dives
down after it. The infuriated spirit-guardians
seize and strangle'him, and his dead body floats
on the surface as a warning,
“Perhaps,” went on my loquacious host, ‘‘you
would go to the land of the Mas-hantoo, the
spirit-gold?” This district, rich in the precious
metal, was cursed by a sultan of old, on account
of the death of his son, and though you may go
there and fill your pockets with gold-dust and
nuggets, they all turn to sand and pebbles when
you cross the boundary on your return.
Meanwhile Morton chafed greatly at our delay,
and I had to exercise much tact to pacify him.
The third evening I saw him in close talk with
the mandor; he then left the boat and went to the
village, returning about dark, with the informa-
tion that we had now permission to proceed.
It seemed strange to me that I had heard
naught about it, but at the time I suspected
nothing. It was a bright moonlight night. Tak-
ing the mandor's kris, Morton went ashore ami
severed the rattan rope where it was tied round
the butt of a tree. The men took their places and
the boat was once more under way
I dropped off to sleep about ten o’clock. I
awoke amidst the crash of boughs and branches,
bringing ruin and destruction on us and our craft.
Although half-stunned, I managed to struggle
from beneath the crushed-in roof, and, as the boat
sank, struck out feebly for the shore, which I
had no sooner reached than I fainted.
What had happened to us was the result of
Morton’s rashness. Poor fellow, he paid for it
with his life. The villagers had not given him per-
mission to proceed, but he had bribed the mandor
to consent to go on at night and risk it. Along
the bank of the river the Dyaks had selected cer
tain leaning trees under which we would pass.
These had been cut through to within an inch of
breaking-point, being secured from falling out-
right by twisted rattans. As we passed, these
guys were cut, and we were swamped by the fall-
ing trees. Morton was killed instantly, although
I strangely escaped, and most of the crew were
more or less hurt. All this 1 learned afterwards.
When I came to my senses I was lying by a fire
in a small clearing in the jungle, with two or three
Dyaks sleeping around. One man was awake,
apparently watching. When .he saw me looking
about he came over to me and brought me a drink,
Ho was very light-coloured, dressed in the ordi-
nary chawat or apron with a jacket, called a
“ lag a,” on his body. He smiled pleasantly and,
addressing me in the native dialect, said “ Saki ”
(a name they had given mo at the village), “ you
were ill-advised to seek the Tampat-mas (gold-
mine) here. Why did you not watch the flight of
the fish-hawk first ?” I asked after Morton, and
he told me of his death.
I was well treated, and the fate of Morton
whom they knew to be guilty of the offence, had
apparently atoned for our trespass.
On the second day I was much recovered, and
the Dyak who had just spoken to me—he was
named Abiasi—was sitting by my side showing me
how to use the blow-pipe, when a strange old
man came from the jungle and advanced in the
clearing.
He was tall and white-bearded, and on his for t ,
head was a round mark made with white pigment
of some sort. Abiasi rose and said something to
him of which I could only catch the word
“ Saghie another name for the forest men. Pre-
sontly the old man, who had only a ragged chawat
on, came over and regarded me earnestly, then he
and Abiasi renewed their conversation.
“Saki,” said the latter at last, addressing me
directly, “If you still wish to see the Tampat-
Mas where the Mas-hantoo is, this old saghie will
take you there.” He then further told me that
the old Saghie, or Orangpooenan, lived in the
mountains where there were many old mines, but
it was all spirit-gold, that turned into sand and
gravel after it was taken away. The Saghie
thought that the presence of a white man might
break the charm. I eagerly agreed to go, and
Abiasi gave me many instructions as to my return,
lent me a parang, or heavy knife, and bade me
farewell.
It was evening when we started and the old man
led me through the jungle by a well-beaten path.
Although the moon was bright, the shadows were
dense where it did not penetrate, and I confess to
feeling very nervous as we pushed on in silence,
starting at intervals some sleeping bird or a troop
of monkeys.
Presently we came to a
small opening and halted
in front of a low-thatched
hut. In answer to his
call a young woman, evi-
dently just aroused from
sleep, came out; she
brought some live embers
and made a fire. Like
the old man, she was
very fair in colour, good-
looking, with well-shaped
limbs, which, as her only
attire was the chawat, or
apron, were fully dis-
played. After eating
some rice and fruit I lay
down by the fire and
slept for the remainder
of the night.
I was not sorry to see
a fine large fish cooking
on the coals for breakfast,
as my returning health
brought with it a good
appetite. When we had
finished our meal the old
man and the girl, whom I
took to be his grand-
daughter, took a large
rush - woven basket be-
tween them and started
along a narrow path lead-
ing through the forest,
motioning me to follow.
In about two miles we
reached an open space,
and before us rose the
rugged side of a hill.
We followed the base of
this round for some time
until the face of the
hill grew steep and pre-
cipitous, and I noticed
we were amongst some
ancient workings.
At the mouth of what
seemed a drive in the
cliff the old Saghie
stopped, and they set
down the basket. He
then spoke rapidly to the
girl, whom he called
Suara, and she collected
dry wood and built up a
fire, the old man lighting
some tinder with a flint
and steel. Suara then
broke down the branches
of a resinous kind of
pine common to the hilly
country, and with the
assistance of my parang
dressed them into rude
torches. I now under-
stood what these pre-
parations meant, and
when we had lit the
torches the i wo picked
up the basket and led the
■way into the tunnel or
drive.
As seen by the dim flare
of the torches, it pre-
sented far more finished
work than any of the
ancient workings I had
yet seen. We must have
gone at least one hun-
dred yards before the old
man stopped, and I then
saw that somebody had
recently been at work,
for there was loose dirt lying about, and some
native tools.
The old Saghie put down the basket, and
motioned to me to come and fill it with the
shovel. I did so, and naturally took the oppor-
tunity of examining the dirt. I sifted some in my
hands, and blew part of the finer dirt away
and am satisfied, even now, that there was a large
quantity of coarse gold through it and several
specimens, as they are generally called by diggers.
Of this I am quite sure, in despite of what
afterwards occurred.
The old Saghie was peering over my shoulder
while I was blowing the dust away, and grinned
hideously as he saw the gold exposed here and
there. I remember wondering at the time what
possible ambition could be his for the yellow dross.
Perhaps he thought the same of me ?
Anyhow, we were both satisfied with our in-
spection, and I went on filling up the bag until it
could hold no more. The old man and the girl
picked it up and carried it out of the tunnel.
Instead of taking the homeward track as I
anticipated they turned down another one, and in
a short time we were beside a small stream that
descended from the range. Here there were rude
appliances for washing, and I selected,_ as the
most convenient, a shallow baked-clay dish, and
commenced washing out a prospect.
Not a speck nor a trace of gold was there. 1
did not look at my two companions, for it struck
me that possibly the dirt at the top of the basket
was different to what I had examined in the


tunnel. I therefore took another prospect from
the very bottom and proceeded to wash it.
That was a strange scene. The narrow path
leading down to the running stream, just cutting
a thin gap in the dense forest. The shrill chatter-
ing and screaming of parrots overhead, and the
noises made by the troops of monkeys, which swung
from bough to bough, and from one long hanging
vine to another. Behind me, as I squatted by the'
water’s edge, the.two yellow, semi-nude figures, of'
the old man and the girl, bending over my
shoulders in rapt attention.
The dirt was rapidly reduced as I swirled the
water round in the dish, and when I tilted it
to and fro, there, at either end of tire grit and
gravel appeared the yellow glow of gold. I
heard the two behind me heave a sigh of satisfac-
tion as this sight appeared. Surely the spell of
the Mas-hantoo was broken at last ?
Suddenly, without a sound of warning, a
glistening flashing object dropped from overhead
and struck me and the girl headlong into the
water. Blinded and frightened, I staggered to
my feet, for the stream was but shallow, and in an
instant saw what had happened. A huge boa had
dropped from a tree above, as is their custom on
the paths by which the deer go to water, and
snatched its victim from our midst. The old man
was crushed against the tree by three or four
folds of the creature whose tail was still in the
branches above, and he was already in the pangs
of death.
Suara, who, like myself, had been knocked for-
ward by a blow from a coil of the reptile as it
dropped on its prey, was standing near me gazing
with horror-stricken eyes on the death-scene.
The crunching of the unhappy man’s bones was
quite audible, but his collapsed body showed that
life was over.
The dish had floated on the surface, and was
now held from going down stream by a tussock of
reeds. Suara picked it up and handed it to me,
with a strange look of despair. Instinctively,
despite the near presence of the monster, now
gloating over its meal, I finished washing the
prospect. The spell of the Mas-hantoo held good.
Nothing but gravel and sand was now in the
earthen dish, which I dashed to pieces on a rock.
Together, Suara I and left the spot and made
our way to the hut, which we reached that even-
ing and there rested for the night. Next morning
she conducted me through jungle paths to within
sight of the village where Abiasi lived.
Here she stopped and pointed in another direc-
tion, nor would she accompany me a step towards
the village, and so, neither able to say farewell to
the other in language both could understand, we
parted. Abiasi told me afterwards that more of
her people lived in the direction she had pointed.
A lot of our goods had been recovered, and the
crew were now nearly all well. A fresh gobang
was provided, and I parted from the Dyak
villagers with strangely mixed feelings, although
it was with some sense of satisfaction that I saw
mile after mile increase the distance between me
and the mines of the Mas-hantoo.
BOOVYEA.
The Sundowner’s Sacrifice.
(PER MR. JACKY DOW.)
Old Jacky gazed on the setting sun
As he stood by his garden gate,
When to him appeared a “ regular” one
Of the sect called the Swag Estate.
“ Chance of a job ?” ’Twas the fixed address,
And it glibly slipped his jaws,
As a parson’s patter as void of stress,
And told, too, for the self-same cause.
An idea lit up the humorist’s brow,
Of a fine retributive plan,
And he answered : “ Well, I want just now
A really active man.
Can you run ? Would you mind running round
the shed ?
You can mess with the men to-night.”
The thought of a feed of yeasty bread
Made the whaler’s heart as light.
He dropped his “hump” and he hitched h
trews,
And took up a hole in his belt;
A stomach ill-used braced up his thews,
His soul two square feeds smelt.
At something like a “ Botany ” pace
He covered the track set out,
Great tears of sweat ran down his face—
He was “ winded, just about.”
As the woolly joker again he faced,
flying a dirty “ wipe,”
A smile that gentleman’s visage graced
Suspiciously rich and ripe.
Ah ! very good. Yes, I think you’ll do
(Give this to the cook) very well.
11 the morning, h —m, I want you to
H—m, clear out of here like—blazes !”

The Story of Conroy’s Gap.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
HIS was the way
of it, don t
you know—
Ryan was
‘ wanted ’ for
stealing sheep
And never a
trooper, high
or low,
Could find him
—catch a wea-
sel asleep !
Till Trooper Scott,
from t li o
l Stockman’s
' Ford—
A tushman,
too, as I’ve
heard them
tell—
Chanced to find him drunk as a lord
Round at the Shadow of Death Hotel.
D’ you know the place ? It’s a wayside inn,
A low grog-shanty—a bushman trap,
Hiding away in its shame and sin
Under the shelter of Conroy’s Gap
Under the shade of that frowning range,
The roughest crowd that ever drew breath -
Thieves and rowdies, uncouth and strange,
Were mustered round at the Shadow of Death.
The trooper knew that his man would slide
Like a dingo-pup, if he saw the chance;
And with half a start on the mountain-side
Ryan would lead him a merry dance.
j
Drunk as he was when the trooper came,
To him that did not matter a rap—
Drunk or sober, he was the same,
The boldest rider in Conroy’s Gap.
“ 1 want you, Ryan,” the trooper said,
“ And listen to mo, if you dare resist
So help me heaven, I’ll shoot you dead !”
He snapped the steel on his prisoner’s wrist,
And Ryan, hearing the handcuffs click,
Recovered his wits as they turned to go,
For fright will sober a man as quick
As all the drugs that the doctors know.
There was a girl in that rough bar
Went by the name of Kate Carew,
Quiet and shy as the bush girls are,
But ready-witted and plucky, too.
She loved this Ryan, or so they say,
And passing by while her eyes were dim
With tears, she said in a careless way,
“ The Swagman’s round in the stable, Jim.”
Spoken too low for the trooper’s ear,
Why should she care if he heard or not ?
Plenty of swagmen far and near,
And yet to Ryan it meant a lot.
That was the name of the grandest horse
In all the district from East to West
In every show-ring, on every course
They always counted the Swagman best;
Ho was a wonder, a raking bay —
One of the grand old Snowdon strain —
One of the sort that could race and stay
With his mighty limbs and his length of rein.
Born and bred on the mountain side,
He could race through scrub like a kangaroo,
The girl herself on his back might ride
And the Swagman would carry her safely
through.
He would travel gaily from daylight’s flush
Till after the stars hung out their lamps,
There was never his like in the open bush.
And never his match on the cattle-camps.
For faster horses might well be found
On racing tracks, or a plain’s extent,
But few, if any, on broken ground
Could see the way that the Swagman went.
When this girl’s father, old Jim Carew,
Was droving out on the Castlereagh
With Conroy’s cattle, a wire came through
To say that his wife couldn’t live the day.
And he was a hundred miles from home,
As flies the crow, with never a track,
Through plains as pathless as ocean’s foam,
He mounted straight on the Swagman’s back.
He left the camp by the sundown light,
And the settlers out on the Marthaguy
Awoke and heard, in the dead of night,
A single horseman hurrying by.
He crossed the Bogan at Dandaloo,
And many a mile of the silent plain
That lonely rider behind him threw
Before they settled to sleep again.
He rode all night and he steered his course
By the shining stars with a bushman’s skill.
And every time that he pressed his horse
The Swagman answered him gamely still.
He neared his home as the East was bright,
The doctor met him outside the town ;
“ Carew ! How far did you come last night ? ” -
“ A hundred miles since the sun went down.”
And his wife got round, and an oath he pass’t,
So long as he or one of his breed
Could raise a coin, though it took their last
The Swagman never should want a feed.
And Kate Carew, when her father died,
She kept the horse and she kept him well;
The pride of the district far and wide,
He lived in style at the bush hotel.
Such \yas the Swagman ; and Ryan knew
Nothing about could pace the crack.
Little he’d care for the man in blue
If once he got on the Swagman’s back.
But how to do it ? A word let fall
Gave him the hint as the girl passed by ;
Nothing but “ Swagman—stable-wall;
Go to the stable and mind your eye.”
He caught her meaning, and quickly turned
To the trooper : “ Reckon you’ll gain a stripe
By arresting me, and it’s easily earned.
Let’s go to the stable and get my pipe ;
The Swagman has it.” So off they went,
And soo f l as ever they turned their backs
The girl slipped down, on some errand bent
Behind the stable, and seized an axe.
The trooper stood at the stable-door
While Ryan went in quite cool and slow,
And then (the tricWiad been played before)
The girl outside jpive the wall a blow.
Three slabs fell out of the stable-wall —
T’was done ’fore ever the trooper knew—
And Ryan, as soon as he saw them fall,
Mounted the Swagman and rushed him through
The trooper heard the hoof-beats ring
In the stable-yard, and he slammed the gate,
But the Swagman rose with a mighty spring
At the fence, and the trooper fired too late,
As they raced away and his shots flew wide
And Ryan no longer need care a rap,
For never a horse that was lapped in hide
Could catch the Swagman in Conroy’s Gap.
And that’s the story. You want to know
If Ryan came back to his Kate Carevv ;
Of course he should have, as stories go,
But the worst of it is, this story's true ;
And in real life it’s a certain rule,
Whatever poets and authors say
Of high-toned robbers and all their school,
These horsethief fellows aren’t built that way.
Come back ! Don’t hope it —the slinking hound,
He sloped across to the Queensland side
And sold the Swagman for fifty pound,
And stole the money, and more beside.
And took to drink, and by some good chance
Was killed— thrown out of a stolen trap.
And that was the end of this small romance
The end of the story of Conroy’s Gap.
THE BANJO.
Johnson and his Mates.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
There were four of us who worked right cut tho
twenty-acre block—
Me and Johnson, Irish Mick, an' Tommy Bates ;
When she buffered out we left the place (the tin-
field, Granite Rock),
’Ceptin’ Johnson —he was wiser than his mates.
Now, we’d not done very badly in the two years
we’d been there
(Fortune’s- bound to come unto the man who
waits),
So we had a spree—a real good time (we’d plenty
cash to spare),
’Ceptin’ Johnson —he was wiser than his mates.
An’ we had a trip to Melbourne, an’ we went an’
saw “the Cup”
(A cup which cheers not, but inebriates).
An’ we all of us backed stiff ’uns—for we played
our money up—
’Ceptin’ Johnson—he was wiser tiiat his mates !
But a jolly time the three of us had while we held
the cash
(Ain't it odd how soon the money dissipates?)
And we showed the city fellers how tin-miners
cut a dash,
’Ceptin’ Johnson—he was wiser than his mates !
For he stayed right on at Granite Rock, did
Johnson, and he lent
All his money out at money-lenders’ rates ;
By-an’-bye his pile grew bigger still—while we’d
lost ev’ry cent —
A r es! that Johnson, he was wiser than his
mates !
Then we thi’ee who’d knocked our substance down
with joyous disregard,
Had to go to graft again, and cuss our fates ;
An’ we worked, and swore, and growled, and
thought our lot was really hard—
’Ceptin’ Johnson —he was wiser than his mates !
lie got rich, an’ drove his carriage, an’ he wore a
shiny hat
On the baldest and the shiniest of pates-;
Wc were getting just our tucker, an’ sometimes
we’d not get that—
Oh, yes; Johnson was much wiser than his
mates !
Then he courted old Brown’s daughter—we were
all in love with her;
But in wooing it’s the “ jink ” that operates ;
An’she married Johnson straight away without
the least demur—
Once more was Johnson smarter than his mates!
Johnson’s wealth kept on increasing, and his
business grew more big,
But the lust for gold is one that ne’er abates ;
And to keep his “specs.” in progress, Johnson
slaved worse than a nig.—
(lie was always reckoned smarter than his
mates !)
Well, the rest of us we jogged along, just in our
usual way
(That is, me an’ Irish Mick, an’ Tommy Bates),
If wo had bad times, we stood ’em—if we’d luck,
we just were gay,
But not Johnson —he was wiser than his mates !
If we struck a patch, an’ made a “rise,” that
“rise” we all enjoyed
(The chummiest we were of syndicates),
An Wed take a spell, an’ see some life (our t-me
was well employed)—
’Ceptin’ Johnson—who was wiser than his
mates!
An’ now Johnson is a J.P., an’ a squatter, an’ a
toff,
An’ his wealth it ev’ry day accumulates —
But he doesn’t know the pleasure now of havine a
week “off,”
Doesn't Johnson—who is wiser than his mates !
An’ he has a “ touch of liver,” an’the gout, like-
wise, he’s got,
An’ his wife’s a reg’lar terror—Johnson states—
But, we're free from wife and liver an’ from gout
an’ all such rot -
’Ceptin’ Johnson—who was wiser than his
mates !
So we think, at times when tucker’s scarce, and
sinkin’s mighty hard,
Or the bottomin’ a duffer aggravates—
That we oughtn’t to be sorry we’re not all from
pleasure barred
Like poor Johnson—who was wiser than his
mates !
For there’s better things in this hero world than
money, you can bet,
(As this story I am telling indicates);
And I’ve come to the conclusion that, in spfto of
riches, yet,
Darned if Johnson was much wiser than his mates /
A. CHEE.


Sydney E. Noose said of somebody the
other day that she had “information of the
brain,” This awful complaint has killed every
Noose man who ever suffered from it.
m RTS is the story of the bumptious Govern-
I meat surveyor, the farmer, and his ‘ dorg,’
The surveyor was prancing round a paddock,
down South, with the air of a man who owned
the earth, and was in treaty for the purchase of
the heavens as well, when the excited owner
came along and told him to “git out o’ that
dam quick.’
The surveyor haughtily replied that he had
been sent there by the Government, and there he would remain until his
business was finished.
“ The Govermint be hanged!” roared the farmer; “that’s my ground, and I
won’t hev no one a-trampin’ across it,” and then the man of measures, flourishing
a roll of plans, declared he held Government papers authorising him to enter the
paddock, and he would stay there as long as he pleased.
Then the farmer spoke never a word nor wept, but, going over to an adjacent
shed, he liberated an aggressive-looking pup.
The ’aughty official and the dog immediately started on a go-as-you-please
flutter for the gate, while the farmer, who evidently had more faith in the dog’s
sagacity than the surveyor, climbed up on a fence to enjoy the fun and yelled, “ Don’t
run away, Mister ! You’ve got authority to stay in that paddock for a year, if you
like. Why don't you shore Tiger them Govermint papers ? Snow him the Gover-
mint papers, y’everlastin’ fool ! STOP AN’ SHOW THE DORG THE
GOVERMINT PAPERS!”
Five minutes later a sad-eyed, panting surveyor might be seen in ihe bush
behind a gum-tree endeavouring to patch the southern hemisphere of his pants
with a parchment-plan and a boot-lace, while a brindled “ dorg ” laid the scalp of
the pants at the feet of his delighted master.
“Not Sufficient Funds.”
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
“Brownson!” called the manager from
the bank-parlour.
“Sir!”
“What’s the amount of Weatherboard’s bal-
ance ?”
“ Thirteen and ninepence..”
“Sure?”
“ Quite.”
And so it was, though I was too lazy to turn up
Weatherboard’s account in the ledger and look.
My indolence was partly hereditary and constitu-
tional, and partly the result of imitating the boss
too closely. He wasn’t a bad fellow, Chapson,
and was very popular, but he was even worse than
myself as regards carelessness. He had a fine,
high-toned, don’t-care-a-curso sort of way of over-
looking such trifles as accuracy and certainty
which quite suited me.
The manager and myself constituted the entire
staff of the Consolidated Bank at Bumblegum. It
was a very slow place, was Bumblegum. Some ’’
days we would have customers, and some days we
wouldn’t; but we never allowed the state of busi-
ness to prey upon our health or undermine our
constitutions. And on slack days we could make
up the books and balance off before closing-time,
and then recreate at billiards or cricket or fishing
from the very moment the doors closed. Nobody
was likely to come in during the last half-hour,,
and if anyone did we would just let the matter go-
in with the next day’s business. This lack of
strict precision on our part never had the effect of
c ausing a crisis, or a financial panic, or an un-
easiness in the London money market, or any
such foolishness, and therefore neither of us-
thought it advisable to worry about strict exacti-
tude.
“Well,” said the manager, “ don’t you cash jny
more of Weatherboard’s cheques. He’s no dam
good. He's gone broke about five times and lie
had the Commercial last year for a hundred-and-
twenty. There’s a cheque of his hero from tho
Provincial for five-pounds that we’ll send back
He don’t get at us, not if I can help it.” Then,
aside: “I want to get quits with him, anyhow.
Took me down for eight-pound-ten at billiards
last night.” And he wrote “N.S.F.” on the back
of Weatherboard’s paper with a grin of satis-
faction. And it was posted for its return journey
to the Provincial Bank at Taramine that evening.
# * *
Next day I was posting up the ledger as
usual, and happened, by accident, to come across
the page devoted to Mr, Weatherboard’s business.
And when I did so, I got a shock worse
than treading on a hoop. For I observed that
Weatherboard’s balance was not 13s. 9d. It was
£l3 9s. And we had dishonoured his cheque fo r
£5 1 And Weatherboard was just the very man
to make things hot for us in consequence.
Well, the boss had to be told, anyhow. So I
coughed away some of the huskiness from ray
throat, and mopped a little of the cold sweat off
my forehead, and went in to the parlour, and told
him.
“Well,” said Chapson, “I must say you are a
•consummate idiot, Brownson.”
I couldn’t deny it. I felt that my superior
officer’s remarks were characterised by solid truth.
He repeated his candid, unbiassed opinion-of my
mental capacity several times with a pleasing
variety of nouns and adjectives. Then he said :
“But we can fix it up, though, now. It’s not
too late. Telegraph to the Provincial, ‘ Weather,
board’s cheque returned in error. Present again.’
Off you go 1 Sharp, now 1”
A great sigh of relief gushed from my overladen
bosom as I realised that we could prevent Wea-
therboard from knowing anything about the
matter, and that there was, after all, no immediate
probability of my being dismissed the bank’s
service for stupidity and culpable negligence. But
when I got to the telegraph-office, I was once
more jerked back to utter misery.
“ Line’s down t’other side of Bingy Creek,’’
said the operator. “Won’t lx; fixed up till the
■day after to-morrow.”
There was only one chance left now. With the
eagerness of a drowning-man grabbing for the
hair of the other fellow, I asked :
“ Has the Taramine mail gone out yet? ”
The cheerfulness of that man was positively
hateful as he replied ;
“Why, bless you, yes ! Mailman started at 7
o’clock this morning. Be at old Gluey’s wine-
shanty by now, boozing up. Don’t think he’ll get
across the creek though, till to-morrow. It was a
banker yesterday, and old Jimmy don’t like water
much, in any shape. Funny old cove is ”
But I didn’t want to hear any more. I was too
miserable. For we were in a fix now, just. I
could see the consequences looming up in the near
future as big as two haystacks. Old Weatherboard
would bring an action against the bank for
damages, as sure as fate, and get them, too. Then
our head-office would want an explanation from
Chapson, and he could only lay the blame on me,
and the result would be that we would both be
dismissed. And all through my accursed laziness.
I could have kicked myself !
When I told Chapson about the telegraph being
interrupted, he swore. First at the repairers,
then at me, then at his condemned luck in having
for an assistant the biggest blankity fool in the
lurid country. I went on with my work and said
nothing. I couldn't. Chapson gave me no time.
He got through with his observations at last
and I could see he was thinking hard. By-and-
bye, his mind seemed to be made up, and he said ;
“I’m going for a ride. If I’m not back by 12
(it was Saturday), lock up and balance off your-
self; that is, if you don’t forget.” This last
remark was ‘‘meant sarcastic.”
* * *
Chapson wasn’t, back at 12 ; and lie didn’t
turn up all the afternoon. And at bed-time, whe n
I inquired at the hotel where he lodged, they said
they had seen nothing of him since morning.
Next day was Sunday, About dusk the
manager rode into town. Ho looked rather the
worse for wear. His horse was nearly knocked
up, too, and had evidently been ridden hard and
long. Chapson said he had chased a kangaroo*
and got lost, and had been out all night in the
ranges. His demeanour, however, was more con-
ciliatory, and his language considerably less crude
than when I last saw him. And a night on the
Bumblegum ranges doesn’t generally improve a
man’s temper either !
* # #
Just before closing time next day, Chapson
called out:
“Hello, Brownson ! here’s a discovery !”
“ What is.it ? ” I asked.
“ Why, here’s that infernal cheque of Weather-
board’s lying on my table! Must have forgotten
to put it in the envelope ! By Heavens, I’m get-
ting careless ! But it’s a lucky thing for us this
time, anyhow. Here, take it, and put it through!’’
Now, I had enclosed that cheque myself and
posted the letter, these being duties the manager
never, under any circumstances, performed. So,
when I had collected my wits a little, I began,
“Why, sir ”
“Now, can’t you dry up ? When will you learn
sense enough to hold your blank foolish tongue’
You ought to know by this time that you only
make an ass of yourself when you do speak. Shut
the door, and when you’ve finished, I’ll give you
30 in a 100 for two nips. Look slippy, now !”
I “dried up.”

Extract from the next issue of the Bumrlh-
gum Scorcher
Mr. James Hobson, who runs the mail from Bumblegum
to Taramine, reports having been stuck up by a buslr
ranger on Saturday last about 16 miles from here. Tin
robber, who was masked, presented a revolver and ordered
Hobson to “bail up.” Hobson, being unarmed, did so,
and his assailant then seized and rifled the Taramine bag.
He then rode off, leaving the letters lying on die road.
The mailman collected them and took them on. From
our respected Postmaster we learn that the robber left all
the letters behind him (being probably disappointed at
their valueless nature) except one. This one bore the
seal of the Consolidated Bank, and the highwayman, n >
doubt, expected that its contents were valuable. But
Mr. Chapson, the able and courteous manager of the Con
solidated Bank here, informs us that the missing letter
was a purely formal one and of no value whatever. The
enterprising knight of the road is now doubtless consider
ing the Question—“ Is bushranging a failure?”
A. C.

*A. WAS the billiard-maker of
® the Gin and Cloves Hotel —
A sandy man, with tender feet, no doubt you
know him well—
Who told this simple story, in his plain,
straightforward way,
Of the disappointed shepherds and the lamb
who went astray.
“ The meanest skunk,”
said Thomas (that’s
the worthy marker’s
name),
“ As ever came in here
to play a dirty,
sneakin’ game,
Was a swell we called
‘ the Kernel ’ —a tol-
lollish breed of bloke,
Who never said ‘ Good
day,’ but what he’d
offer one a smoke.
“ You must know that
this ’ere Kernel used
to lounge about the
room,
A talking of his ’orses,
and his kerridges,
an’ groom;
He didn’t sport no jool-
rey except a slap-up
ring,
And if we had a Pool
he’d drop his shil-
lings like a king.
“ Three months or so I’d I hi
know’d ’im, when I
put it to the chaps
As the Kernel might be good to work a swindle
on, perhaps,
Tor he give me the impression that he fancied
he’d a show
To hold his own at billiards with old Chorley
Clitheroe.
“ Old Chor —there ain’t his equal for a puttin’
through a toff —
Had been ’avin friendly games with Mister
Kernel on and off ;
And when the Kernel beat him then the old ’un
used to kid,
As he hadn’t got no nerve for playin’ billiards
like he did.
“So the boys they sit and watch ’em, O ! so quiet
and subdood
And remark, in stagey whispers, as the Kernel
was ‘ too yood,’
And how he seemed a gentleman wot wasn't up
to snuff,
Or else he’d land old Chorley for a tidy lump o’
stuff.
‘ But the Kernel didn’t seem to want to have no
money down ;
At first the most he played for was a modest
’arf-a-crown,
And when the game was over, let him win or let
him lose,
He was sure to ask the company to order in their
booze.
“ Yer can’t make brass by drinking —we was
almost in despair
Of gettin’ at our juggins with the ’igh and
mighty air—
When at last he says to Chorley: ‘Will you
play me for a stake ?’
And Chorley, after kiddin’, said he would—-‘for
