EDMUND FISHER.
“In Memory of
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Jimberoo, on the Upper Meroe, Never-
Never Land, has always been reckoned a hot
place —in more senses than one. It’s temperature
was nearly always a hundred in the shade, when
it wasn’t a hundred and ten, neither grade being
conducive to strict abstention from alcoholic drinks
in a place whore there was little better to do than
hoist one’s toes on the arms of a “ squatter’s” chair
and discuss the prospect of being able to get away
for the next “Cup.” If by any chance the ther-
mometer took an occasional deviation either below .
or above its two normal points, then it was felt
that the occasion required celebrating with more
copious libations than usual, and when it rained,
which happened once in two years, the entire
township got drunk with tho greatest promptitude,
and remained in that condition till the heavens
had resumed their normal placid aspect. The
somewhat nomadic population of squatters,
drovers, bank-officials and Government hacks, to
say nothing of the doctor and lawyer, were all
pretty well “seaspned,” but we were not altogether
without aspirations towards better things, for one
Sunday long Sandy M’Dorrald, the overseer at
Warribridi, having a very sore head and remem-
bering the days when he would gang to kirk at
hatne, tried to get up a “call” to some saintly
body of elders away south to send along a parson.
“ And they’re rare ylns for the whuskie, mon,
some of these Presbyterian meenisters,” he added
as an extra inducement. But as, after discussing
the matter till the original subject was almost for-
gotten, we could only guarantee an annual stipend
fund of nineteen pounds seven shillings and three-
pence, we were forced to abandon the idea.
The arrival of a new resident was always an
■event at Jimberoo. Consequently, when a fresh
accountant arrived at one of the local banks, vve
■gave him as hearty a welcome as we had given his
predecessor a hearty send-off the week before.
“ Hardly up to our mark, but not a bad sort,’’
was the verdict.on John Marchland. Ho was a
little man, placid of speech and mild of manner,
with kindly eyes and a good matured face. Ho
had not been long in the bank and was about to
keep himself on the munificent salary of three
pounds a week, and not only keep himself but—
Heaven help him ! —a wife.
If John March laud was little and quiet his wife
was certainly large and loud. They had been
married a month, and'she was the daughter of his
boarding-house keeper in B . A full-blown
rose with a complexion liberally got up and a style
of dress that made flash Harry, the drover, ejacu-
late, “ Well, this is a stunner !” it was easy to see
that John Marohland’s married life would pro-
bably at times be stormy, and it was also equally
easy to see that he worshipped the very ground his
wife trod on.
Tor two or three months, except in the way of
business, we saw little of the new bank-clerk.
Our ways were apparently not his ways, he shrank
from the constant invitations to drink or play a
hundred up, or “ put a shilling in and th« winner
shouts,” pursuits of which the common or bachelor
bank-clerk apparently never tires ; he always
returned to his cottage immediately the bank
closed and was rarely seen later.
Tew could help noticing that his face got
careworn as time went on, and there were
rumours, traceable, of course, to the ten-bob-a-
week servant, that matters were not going
smoothly in the Marchland household.
“The missis wus a-givin’ it dm awful last night,
she wus,” confided this young lady to her bosom
friend next door ; “ poor dear man, ’es as quiet as
a blessed lamb, but she’s a reg’lar she-devil, and
they do say, too, as she ain’t no better nor ” —and
here the conversation went off into private and
probably slanderous possibilities; but. however
this may be, it was evident that to John March
land the vulgar, tawdry, over-dressed woman he
had taken for better or for worse was a goddess to
be loved indeed. One had only to notice the
almost ludicrous look of pride and affection with
which he watched her every movement, to be sure
of this.
In the meantime, as the year passed, things went
from bad to worse, for Marchland began to drink.
It was not that ho drank regular.y and “on
principle,” as did the rest of us. But now and
then he would come into the parlour of Host Mike’s
hotel and, sitting alone, would drink silently till
the potions, acting on an unaccustomed brain,
rendered him oblivious to all his troubles, and we
knew that on these nights his wife would probably
have left him alone afier a violent quarrel, and
that he was drinking to forget, if possible. It
was the old story of a man driven by an over-
mastering Tate.
One evening, when John Marchland had
been at Jimberoo barely a twelvemonth, be re-
returned home, looking more careworn and wor-
ried than ever; and with reason, for his pockets
were full of unreceipted bills ; and although the
up-country storekeeper is more than lavish with
his credit, still there comes a time when even his
patience gives out.
“John,” she said, “I must have twenty-five
pounds. ’
“ Good God, Annie, you know I have not five
pounds in the world ; and there is no one I could
get it from.”
He was wrong there, for almost any of the
“boys” would have got him the money; but he
would not ask.
“But I tell you I must. It’s no use telling me
that you are in debt and cannot get it. Your own
fault, for being in such a miserable place. Twenty-
five pounds I must have by to-morrow, or I will
leave you for ever.”
John Marchland clutched at his wife’s dress.
“Don’t say that, Annie, don't say that—any-
thing but that, dear.”
“ But Ido say it. Get me the money, or I will
never see you again.” And, with a bang of the
door, she left him.
At the bank next day it was very plain that
John Marchland had been drinking heavily— so
much so that he barely knew what he was doing.
To himself it seemed that on every cheque, on
every bill, on every page of the ledger, he could
see nothing but the sum o: twenty-five pounds.
The overdrafts in thousands, the accounts in
hundreds, the fat balances of the local butcher
and storekeeper all bore their relation to this
wretched account of twenty-five pounds—a
miserable sum, indeed, but how far beyond his
reach ! As the day wore on, his burning head and
throbbing eyeballs (for lie had been drinking even
in the bank) .could no longer bear the strain ;
dazed with drink and care, he was about to close
the books and go, when How Long, the gardener,
came in to make a deposit. It was twenty-five
pounds, exactly, in notes.
Half-an-hour afterwards John Marchland came
to his h me.
“Annie,” he said, holding out his hand, “ here
is the money—kiss me ”—and he staggered on to
the conch arid fell into a drunken sleep.
When he awoke he could remember nothing at
first, but it was plain that he had slept long, for
the sun had risen on a glorious summer morning.
. Then he caught sight of a letter placed con-
spicuously on a table near, it was addressed in his
wife’s handwriting and, tearing it open, he read
the contents.
With the money you have given me I am starting by
the night coach to B . You can send me from time to
time what money I shall require.—Annie.
Then he remembered everything. Deserted, and,
not only deserted, dishonoured too, it came sud-
denly to the man that he had only one alternative
left, the usual resource of those too weak to
wrestle with this rough world. Placing his wife’s
photograph before him on the table, he wrote on
its back a last message.
Good-bye, dear wife, you will get the insurance-money
and can pay back the bank. Good-bye, dear.
A moment later, two clicks of tiie trigger and John
Marchland was dead.
* * #
Twelve months afterwards we were dis-
cussing in our usual lazy manner all possible topicf
—from the fate of Europe to the winner of the
Cup—when the mail came in. One of our number,
who had secured a copy of the leading B daily,
suddenly startled us with a profane ejaculation,
He held out the paper in silence—we all looked,
and in the obituary column was the following
notice :
MARCHLAND.—In memory of John Marchland, who
departed this life Kith August, ISS—.
“Thou art not yet forgotten, dear,
Although the days are dark and drear
Without thee.”
Inserted by his sorrowing and loving widow, Annie.

THE JOYS OF POULTRY-PA RMING.


TERRIBLE TOMMY: THE BOY BUSHRANGER.
A SUNDAY SCHOLAR’S STORY.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.)
CHAPTER I. —THE ROBBERY.
■ / ? Hp=p
WAS evening.
The sad, sweet glam-
our of twilight was
settling down slowly
but surely on Wombat
Gully.
The tall gum-trees growing on either side of the
r oad that ran through the Gully whispered weirdly
to each other in the solemn stillness, like the
muffled conspirators in a melodrama.
But no sound came from the dark river running
thousands of feet below; perhaps because ther®
was no river; perhaps because a passing com
positor had drunk it up. The true reason wil
probably never be known.
It was a savage scene, worthy of the pencil of
Salvator Rosa, who would delight in painting it
as a drop-curtain for the Minstrels if ho were not,
unfortunately, dead.
Some such thought as this would, no
have been passing through the mind of the soli,
tary figure sitting on a log by the side of the road
if he —for the figure was human had ever heard
of Salvator Rosa.
But he had not—a sad evidence of the results
of irreligious education.
* * #
His face was pale ; his eyes dark and
mournful, but with something in them that told
the observer they could on occasion dart a glance
as deadly as the basilisk’s. On his head he
wore a cap of curious construction which looked
strangely like an old hat with the brim cut off.
He said it was the scalp of a schoolmaster.
His other attire consisted of a coat and mole,
skin —he called it policeman-skin —trousers, and
two odd boots, several sizes too large for him,
which he referred to as mocassins.
In his belt were stuck what appeared to be a
carving-knife and a handsaw.
He said they were “ yataghans.”
Across his knees lay a shotgun, with an
enormous barrel, which he stroked caressingly,
from time to time addressing it as “ Grave-filler,”
“Corpse-maker,” “Coroner’s Joy” and other
cheerful names of that kind.
He seemed to have a grim humour and fine play
of fancy for one so young.
For he could not have been more than twelve
years old at the most. And yet—but we are
anticipating. What brought this small boy out
so late in such a gloomy spot ?
’Possum-shooting, perhaps.
Ha! Ha !
# « *
Suddenly the silence was broken by the
sound of wheels.
It was the coach from Broken Hill bowling
gaily along with a cargo of bearded diggers on
board bound for Adelaide with the intention of
painting that quiet city a lurid vermilion before
they left it.
There were eight horses in front. It had been
found necessary to use so many on account of the
weight of gold—mostly in nuggets of the size of a
cottage-loaf—which the diggers would insist on
bringing down with them.
The driver was the celebrated “Brummy”—a
reckless desperado who had once shot a man who
differed from him over the spelling of a word, but
was pardoned for his marvellous skill as a “ whip.’’
On each side of him sat a trooper, armed to the
teeth, who had been sent to guard the coach—
which also contained a chest of golden ingots sub-
scribed by the generous miners of Broken Hill to
the Victorian Temperance Society, care of Mr. S.
A. King, editor of the Licensed Viotuaeleu*
They had been jesting with “Brummy” about
the absurdity of having an escort on such a seeir 0
road, when, as the coach came to the top of ti e
hill and was plunging into the gloom of the gully,
he gave a wild whoop and threw the reins of the
eight horses into the air, catching them dex-
terously as they came down with his teeth.
Anyone who could have
seen the troopers at that
moment would have
noticed that the bronzed
hue of their cheeks had
turned to a livid green.
‘ ‘ Brummy ” chuckled
softly to himself. But
suddenly as he caught
sight of the figure sitting
by the side of the road
it was his turn to look
green.
“ It’s all up with us,
gentlemen,” he muttered
with a savage impreca-
tion, for “Brummy,”
being the best driver on
the road, naturally swore
up to that level and some-
times cousiderab’y above
it.
“ What's up :” asked
the troopers, who bad
begun to recover their
courage somewhat.
“Everything, ■
you ! Do you see any-
thing on the. side of the
road there ?”
“ Yes, a boy—wait-
ing to get a lift very
likely.”
“Waitin’ to get a lift.
Oh, ye-',” and he laughed
bitterly; “ waitin’to get
a lift of what’s in this yer
coach, and you, too, if
yer not mighty civil.”
“Why, who is it?” asked the astonished
troopers.
“Who is it,” replied “Brummy,” who had a
habit of repetition when he wanted to be parti-
cularly contemptuous.
“ It’s Terrible Tommy - that’s who it is.”
* # *
The troopers laughed incredulously and
said, “Just leave him to us. We’ll soon settle
him.*’
“All right, gentlemen, but I’d better tell you
before it’s too late that he’s sent more o’ your kind
to where there ain’t no free drinks than you’re
years old. But if you will tackle him do it an’ I’ll
try to ’old the ’osses. I’ll probably see you both
again where you’ll be the chief features of th®
entertainment, as the play-bills say.”
“Where might that
be?” they enquired.
“ The Coroner’s in-
quest ” —with a grim
chuckle.
These remarks had been Interchanged rapidly
and the coach was now within about a hundred
yards of the small motionless figure.
“Perhaps the poor boy’s asleep,” said one
trooper, loosening his revolver.
“ Or dead,” muttered the other, doing the same.
“ Brummy ” merely snorted.
Suddenly as the loading horse came within a
dozen yards of him the
figure sprang into the
road and pointing his
shotgun at the driver,
said in a sweet treble,
“Have the goodness (he
was always polite, was
Tommy) to stop this
vehicle for me for a few
moments.”
“Why, what’s the
matter, Tommy ?” ex-
claimed “Brummy” in
a wheedling tone, doing
as he was told, neverthe-
less.
“ Now, tell those gen-
tlemen beside you to be
kind enough to step
down,” continued the
voice, ignoring the ques-
tion.
“Do, or you’re bleed-
ing corpses,” whispered
Brummy, “ he was never
known to miss his man
yet.”
But the troopers had
too much reputation at
stake to obey without the
show of a struggle.
Both blazed away at
the same moment.
The horses plunged a little, but Brummy held
them with wrists of steel.
Tommy laughed scornfully and Stood there urn
moved and uninjured. Just then one of the
diggers inside looked out to see what was the
trouble. , ...
“ What is it, Bill ?” enquired those inside,
“ Only a kid m the road bailm up the coach
he growled.
" Go out and kick him,” they suggested.
“ He’s got a gun,” said Bill drily, “and a boy
with a gun is as good as a man while his ammuni-
tion lasts. Perhaps one of you fellows would like
to go out ?”
“ What’s the gun like?” cautiously enquired the
man who had made the suggestion.
“ Looks like a shotgun with a mighty big barrel,”
was the reply.
* * *
While this conversation had been going on
among the passengers Tommy stepped forward
and cried in short, sharp, but still melodious tones,
‘ Enough of this nonsense, if you please. My
time is valuable. I have not the good fortun e
to be a member of the force.” This tickled
“ Brummy ” so much that he nearly let go the
reins with laughing. “ Gentlemen,” said the
diminutive brigand, “ I give you thirty seconds
to consider what you are going to do. You ob-
serve you are covered.” Then a wonderful thing
happened. The huge barrel of the shot-gun
opened out like a fan into little wicked-looking
barrels that ranged round the whole of the com-
pass so that the small desperado looked like the
hub of a wheel.
The troopers got down.
“ Deposit your revolvers there,” indicating a
place a few yards away. “ Now go and stand in
that spot of moonlight.”
They did.
“ And now, gentlemen,” he continued, address-
ing the passengers, “ I am sorry to put you to
this temporary inconvenience, but please step out
one by one and stand beside those gallant officers.
First, however, inform me if you have a lady
inside.”
They replied in the affirmative, and a stout, old
lady carrying a basket came forth.
It contained eggs, she said, which she was taking
in to the Melbourne market. “ Good young gen.
tleman,”she cried, falling on tier knees before
him, “ don’t rob me. I’m a poor lone widow with
nothing but this little basket of eggs.”
“ Say no more, good mother,” said the gallant
boy, “ you and your eggs are safe from me. It
shall never be said that Terrible Tommy ever in •
jured a woman. I only regret that the exigencies
of my situation prevent my emulating the example
of my distinguished predecessor, Claude Duval,
and asking you to do me the favour of joining me
in a minuet on the greensward—l mean the road_
But, perhaps, I may have that happiness another
time. Meanwhile, just step out and sit down on
the bank there while I settle with these gentle-
men. I won’t detain you long.”
The old lady got out and sat down with her
precious basket of eggs in her lap.
Tommy knew as well as she did that the
“ eggs ” were nuggets, but he was too courteous
to let her suspect this.
Tommy never forgot he was a gentleman.
The diggers got out after her and stood in
a row with the two troopers.
“ My valiant friends,” said Tommy, addressing
the latter, “you will do me a further favour if one
of you will step inside the coach once more and
push out to the other end a small chest you will find
inside. It is addressed in care of a friend of mine>
and I will take charge of it for him till he calls
for it.”
In the course of a few minutes the box was in
the road.
“Now, then,” said Tommy, gracef. lly placing
one foot upon it and addressing the diggers, “I
wish to say a few farewell words to you. You
may think my conduct strange and perhaps even
uncalled-for on this occasion. But you will ba
grateful to me in after times when you come to
consider the matter calmly. This is what,
when I attended school in the vanished past,
my teacher—poor old man ! 1 had to shoot him ;
he would be so exacting in the matte :■ of
long-division used to call an object lesson.
You were on your way to Melbourne when
I, fortunately for all of us, stopped you.
You were going to throw your money away in
foolishness and, perhaps, worse. You would have
been robbed anyhow, so that you see I am really
your benefactor in limiting your immoral courses.
You will now kindly step forward and each de-
posit half of his money in a line with the door of
the coach and then enter with my bhssing. . I
trust to your honour not to deceive me in the
amount you deposit.”
Touched by this genei’ous conduct each digger,
as he passed by, dropped about a tenth of what
he carried in the spot indicated and got into the
coach again.
Tommy was in too great a hurry to get away
with the box—a fortune in itself —to let on that
he knew this. As the old woman passed by with
her basket he took up the largest nugget in the
pile and handed it to her saying with a smile,
“ Good mother, allow me to add another egg to
your basket.”
Covered with confusion she stumbled in with
’ he others, and then, turning to the two troopers,
Tommy said to them, “I shall not ask you for
any money, gentlemen, but as I have put you to
some inconvenience I beg you will each accept the
largest nugget you can find in the pile as a small
contribution to the building of your first terrace.
/ Tl d now, good-bye, and convey to your friends of
the Police Department my best wishes. Get along
V> rummy.”
* * *
As the coach disappeared down the road
the passengers could still see the small figure of
the young bandit standing in a path of moonlight
in the same position with one foot on the box. .
Presently he gave a cat-call and four or five
figures of about his own size emerged from the
moonlight.
‘ ‘ All right, Cap’n ? ” said one interrogatively.
“All right ?” recreated Tommy contemptously_
“Did ycu ever know me to fail? Take that box
aw.iy and place it in the old spot.”
When they had gone Tommy shut the gun up
again and laughed. “To think the blessed fools
' could be frightened by a lot of stair-rods.” But
in a moment his eyes assumed the old mournful
expression and he sighed, “This is an exciting
pf e —b u t I would give that chest of gold for a slice
of bread and jam.” Then passing his hand across
his brow he murmured fiercely, “Perish these
maddening memories !” and strode after his com-
panions into the darkness.
* * #
Tommy and his band were captured that
night in their cave. One of their number—a
dastard by the name of Billy Jinks —had betrayed
them. Tommy swore, while the deadly basilisk
light glittered in his eyes, that even if he had to
wait a hundred years for
it he would get even
with Billy Jinks.
And he did—but ol
that anon.
Terrible Tommy was
not taken without a des-
perate struggle. With
his own hand he slew
four of his assailants—all
strong and powerful men.
As the fourth fell with a
dull thud on the rocky
floor of the cave Tommy
heard the sound as of
broken glass, and a strong
odour of rum pervaded
the atmosphere.
Ho looked down and
discovered that the noise
and the smell came from
a bottle in the pocket of
the deceased, which had
been smashed in his fall.
A rapid combination
of familiar recollections
caused him to turn over
the face of his slain
enemy.
He staggered and gasp-
ing wildly “ Oh, heaven,
I have killed my old
man !” fell backward into
the arms of a policeman.
# # #
Weeks after when Tommy came out to be
hanged, the good chaplain asked him if he had
anything to say before he was worked off.
“Yes,” he replied, in a solemn whisper, “tell
all the boys you know who stay away from Sunday-
school and want to be bushrangers to take warn-


mg by me and repent in time. ”
And the good chaplain said he would.
Then the white cap vas drawn over Tommy’s
eyes, there was a sudden jerk, and he awoke with
his father bending over him.
He knew it was his fat hr because of the spiri-
tuous smell of his breath.
“Then I’ve not been hanged?” murmured
Tommy, while the cold sweat of terror stood thick
upon his brow.
“No, but you will be one of these days if you
don’t alter your ways,” replied that respectable
parent; “get up out of this at once and fetch me
a shillingsworth of rum and tell them to put it
down with the other lot. ”
Tommy rose up, and as he did so a book fell out
of his bed. It had a flaming-pictured cover re-
presenting a boy of about his own age with a re-
volver in each hand and a proud smile of defiance
on his lip standing upon the bodies of two police-
men and keeping four others at bay. It was
entitled “ Dare-Devil-Dick—the scourge of the
Never-Never. ’ He had fallen asleep reading it
the night before.
“ After this,” said Tommy, with earnest fer-
vour as he hid the bottle for his parent’s rum
under his waistcoat, “ I’ll turn good boy and go
to Sunday-school regularly—if it kills me. But I
know that part of my dream about Billy Jinks
must mean something. I never thought much of
him, anyhow.”
# * *
Five little years rolled away and Terrible
Tommy was in a Land-Boomer’s office—the smug-
gest, sleekest, most respectable of clerks—and
teaching regularly in a Sunday-school.
One day he met Billy Jinks, who had lately
come into some money, and, taking him aside,
said confidentially, “ Billy, would you like to go
in for a big thing ’”
“ Would I not ? ” answered Billy with emphasis.
“ Then I can put you in for it—l do it for the
sake of old times, Billy, because hundreds of
people would give their ears to be put in for it.”
So Billy went in for it to the extent of ad-
vancing Tommy pretty well all the money he had
received as a legacy.
“ You’ll wake up a millionaire one of these
days, Billy,” said Tommy, asking him to have “ a
bottle of fizz ” out of his own money.
Tommy invested the coin all right, and sold the
property for eight times its value a month after-
wards.
It teas a good thing. Tommy had told the
truth so far.
But that was the last Billy saw of his money.
Tommy has an office of his own, and is now one
of the most promising young business-men in
Melbourne, and everybody says he has a great
future before him because he is so religious and
so steady.
One day a forlorn-looking creature with touzled
hair and a hungry look in his eyes came to
Tommy’s office.
“ Well,” said Tommy, in his brisk business way,
“ what do you want ? ”
“ Don’t you know me, Tommy ? ” said the for-
lorn creature in broken tones.

A Journalist’s Funeral.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It was a new sensation to be in a coffin, with a
hearse all to myself as the sole passenger. I had
exhausted all other sensations, and this seemed to
be the last. Strangely enough I was not afraid—
the utterly novel experience acted upon me like
hasheesh. I actually enjoyed it in a dreamy con-
templative kind of way. You will at once per-
ceive that I was not dead at the time, or I would
not be writing this now.
Two dismally shabby cabs crawled after my
conveyance, and I noticed with mingled envy and
indignation (so far as I could be touched by eithe r
of these feelings) that they stopped at every hotel
on the road—in order that the mourners might
have an opportunity ot drinking my health, I
suppose.
In the state of trance—for that was what it
really was—in which I was at the time the mys-
terious faculty, sometimes vaguely called the sixth
sense, must have been developed. I heard what
my mourners were saying about me as plainly as
if I had been sitting beside them, while the hearse
went slowly grinding along the dusty road to the
cemetery.
‘Me was nobody's enemy but his own—poor
•Tack !” said Mr. O’Connell, blowing through the
stem of his pipe to clear it. And out of the glass
windows of the hearse—paid for, as I discovered
afterwards, by a generous theatrical manager
whom I have written slightingly of on many
occasions, but who, thank heaven, still survives to
be repaid by my borrowing a tenner from him the
first time we meet—l saw, through all intervening
obstacles, this stout, dark b'ttle man making rings
(mourning rings, I suppos* vith his tumbler on
the bar-counter of the hotel.
And at once there flashed upon me a memory.
It was a moonlit night in summer six years ago—
ah, those happy days when I was so miserable !
I was on board one of the small steamers that
run from Circular Quay to Mossman’s Bay. Sit-
ting beside me in a great grey coat which, with
his head peering forward predaciously, so to
speak, gave him the appearance of a bald-headed
eagle, was the brightest and lightest writer I
have met in these lands. He was not given to
serious reflection as a general thing, and I was
somewhat surprised when he turned to me sud«
denly and said in a tone of gloom so intense that
it was almost grotesque : “ They may say any-
thing they please about me when I am dead, so
long as they don’t say ‘He was nobody’s enemy
but his own.’” And that is precisely what they
did say, word for word. He has been dead for
some years now, but his imitators have been many
and one or two have almost succeeded in catching
something of the ring of his style—reminding me
of the tall skeleton in Goethe’s ballad who is
climbing up the steeple of a church to get at the
sexton, and, when he has almost attained the top,
falls to the ground, a heap of bones, the moment
the cock crows.
We —that is to say, the hearse, the under-
taker’s men and myself—were about a mile ahead
of my mourners just then. I noticed them
coming out of the fourth public-house on the
road wiping their lips, and then the two disgrace-
ful shandridans came tearing along, full-gallop,
after me. I also noticed that there was another
funeral approaching by a cross-street. There
seemed to be no end to it. I remembered that
it was the final procession of a prominent old
pirate who had died a few days before me, and
had been kept in state (on ice) till then. He had
risen to affluence and a title by usury during
six days of the week, and carrying the
plate around in churches on
the seventh. He had most
of the city folio wing him to
where he was going to sleep
in hopes of a glorious resur-
rection. And I had only
a wretched queue of two after
me—a, man of poetry and
learning—a man wh j had
hauled upon the Archimedean
| wer till it almost snapped
with the strain ! It was dis-
gusting. If I could have
only got out of my coffin and
on to the box-seat of the
hearse I would have made a
vigorous dash—the proces-
sions being somewhat mixed
■—to divert the traffic and as-
sume the lead of the retired
pirate’s cortege and let my two shabby cabloads
go to mourn over him!
But they had overtaken us by this time—all
, , ~ ... ~
talking together and smoking like chimneys.
“ Well, there’s no use Crying over spilt milk,
boyg » ( nobo dy was crying that I could see, so this
. ,
must have been a mere facon de purler) said the
sub-editor of the paper to which 1 used to contn-
bute most of my work. “ He’s dead, and that’s
ad t bere ; s about it. Brush away those tears !
(Spoken melodramatically—there were no tears.)

I’ve got to get out here to start on my work, dead
or no dead.”
Which he at once proceeded to do, and went on
his way with long strides and a slight lurch.

Plent y where’s he’s going,” said M‘Nab, the
commercial man, “ he always liked his liquor hot.’'
And then this perjured calumniator of the dead
began to tell a string of the most outrageous
fictions concerning what he and I had done when
we had been on “ tears ” -together—things which,
if they had ever happened, would have got us into
gaol or the lunatic-asylum half-a-dozen times over.
Yet, he actually intended these astounding fabri-
cations as a tribute to my memory ! It was his
idea of a funeral eulogium.
“Well, gentlemen,” said the first voice, “I
must leave you here. I enjoy a spree as much as
anybody, but I have some special work to do, and
I can’t neglect it. See the old boy planted truly
and well.”
And he went off. “Nice lot of heart-broken
mourners I have, ” I thought.
My old friend, Tom Dorgan, who had never
said a word all this time, except that it was a fine
day for a funeral, then observed, “ The mourners
are thinning out, Flynn.”
“Yes,” replied Flynn (a kindly cynic in his
way), “ they mostly do at the end of. any funeral
of this sort. You see ” —(it was an amiable char-
acteristic of Flynn’s that when he had an audience
cooped up in a corner —and what better corner
could be found than a mourning cab ?—he held
that audience at his mercy, which wasn’t much)—■
“the poor old boy had to die. He didn’t time his
drinks, you know. All the time was his time
when liquor was about ” (another atrocious per-
jury), “ and ’’—jerking his thumb in my direction
—“he has made all his time his own by it. Besides,
the strain upon his intellect in writing comic para-
graphs was enough to kill an emu. I’m beginning
to feel it tell upon me, myself. ”
At this there was a muffled laugh—out of
respect to the feelings of Flynn, I suppose.
“ I want to get out at South Melbourne,” said
a husky voice from the corner of the cab.
It appeared that this gentleman had got into
one of the cabs while the mourners and drivers
were in a pub., saying what a fine fellow I was if
I could only have kept sober one day a month or
so, and being in a somewhat intoxicated condi-
fcion, had mistaken it for one of the cabs that plied
on the road to where he lived.
“ I’d like to inform you, sir, that you are at a
funeral, and respect shall and will be shown to the
illustrious dead, or there will be trouble,” said
M‘Ginnis Walker, of the Press Bureau, for the
first time. And with that M‘Nab hustled the
gentleman violently into the road. As soon as he
had picked himself up he tore off his coat, threw
his hat down, and challenged the entire funeral,
offering to smash up the whole party and dance
upon the corpse (regarding which he made certain
coarse remarks that almost caused me to burst
through my trance with rage).
But the funeral passed on and left him prancing
around in a ring and defying everything within
the circle of the horizon to come on and be ground
into powder.
Soon after another mourner left. He said ho
would be late for dinner if he didn’t get out it
once and his wife would be very angry if such a
tragedy occurred.
And he departed.
‘‘ He was not a bad fellow—he was my best
friend, boys—this has cut me up terribly—have
you got a match, Flynn ?” said M‘Nab, the com-
mercial man.
“He stole two of my best original stories,"
growled M'Ginnis Walker, “but I forgive him,
now he’s dead. I stole them myself.”
When we arrived at the grave there was only
one mourner—M'Ginnis Walker, and he was
asleep in a corner of the cab. All the rest bad
either got out or fallen out on the way. Tom had
been overcome by his grief at the last pub., and
M‘Nab had been taken in charge by a policeman
for offering in a public place to fight anybody
who would dare to say that I wasn’t the best all-
round genius that had lived since the time of
Shakespeare.
I felt two or three dull thuds on my coffin-lid
and awoke with a jump.
-“ Mother of Glory!” I yelled, “ what’s this ? ”
About a yard of the bedroom ceiling had fallen
upon me. That was all.
I wonder if this will be anything like what will
occur when I die ? I don’t care in the least so long
as, in the words of my friend gone before, they
don’t say, “He was Nobody’s Enemy but his
Own. ”
The Barmaid
Ther
Boy
YOUNG FREDDY stands before the bar
Whence all but he have fled;
The smoke from Freddy’s big cigar
Forms circles round his head.
A rose-bud blushes on his coat,
His heart is full of joy,
And many simple signs denote
He is a Barmaid’s “ Boy.”
Behind the bar Miss Flossy stands;
The girl is slim and dark,
And washing glasses gives her hands
A kind of ruddy bark ;
But ah ! she is a graceful dear,
So fresh and trim and smart:
Not stout and bitter like the beer —
Although a little “ tart.”
Woung Freddy stops a solid hour
To gaze in Flossy’s eyes,
And soon the rosebud from his coat
Upon her bosom lies ;
He swallows half-a-dozen drinks
With rapture and delight,
At last the wearied syren thinks
“ It’s time to say good-night.”
His fingers Flossy’s fingers press—
How thrilling is their touch !
He’d like to snatch a fond caress,
But Flossy knows too much.
(Of all the womenkmd we meet,
There’s none can be so coy,
So altogether quite discreet,
As barmaids with a Boy.)
Young Freddy takes a fond farewell,
And struts away on air
(Who knows the power of barmaid’s spell,
Unless he has “ been there ”?)
Another Boy comes strolling in
As Freddy turns his back ;
That rosebud, fastened with a pin,
Brings happiness to Jack.
Then Flossy hears it all again,
For Jack is also young,
And sings the old familiar strain
Which foolish Fred has sung ;
And, after Jack, on Dick and Tom
Her arts she must employ—
There is no rest for Flossy from
The ardent Barmaid’s Boy.
They come and go, they go and come—
Boys dull, or bright and quick :
She hardly gives a thought to some—
The others turn her sick;
Mechanical the witching glance
She sheds on all the batch,
And Floss ignores each decent chance
To make a decent match.
She might have Bertie if she chose,
The notion makes her smile ;
And as for Freddy, goodness knows
He’s not at all her style;
Such youngsters may be straight and true,
As some among them are—
They haven’t learned the way to woo
Young ladies at the bar.
For all their roses and their gloves
The girl cares not a jot,
One man, in truth, the syren loves,
A lazy, reckless sot;
Pert, pretty Flossy’s peace of mind
Her “ fancy ” will destroy—
The Barmaid is at least as blind
As any Barmaid’s Boy.
And, doubtless, after many days
(When Freddy and the rest
Have settled down to sfiber ways
And gi> en barmaids best),
To Floss, in Time’s hard lesson schooled,
This thought will oft occur :
That, while her Boys she lightly fooled,
Her “chap ” was fooling her.
EDMUN D FIRSHER.


The Last of the Wombat,
Barge.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Chapter I.
Jim Killen, the puntman, and his mate
Tom had just swung back their pontoons after
-letting the old Pride of the Murray and her
barges drop through from the wharf, when the
exhaust of an incoming steamer was borne pant-
ingly on the languid evening air. Jim put his
hand to his ear to catch the sound more distinctly.
Like most of the dwellers by the river-side he
could distinguish the various boats by the tones of
their escaping steam.
“It’s the little Lizzie,” he growled to his
assistant. “S’pose I’ll have to let her through !’’
“’T’aint the Lizzie, Jim. It’s Maltby’s Hero.’’
“ Get alon’ with you, you Sydney-side crow.
Think I don’t know ev’ry twist and turn of the
little woman’s steampipe. Listen there now—
she’s down by the park, and she always speaks so
when she’s that far. ‘ Open the bridge, Jim ;
open the bridge, Jim !’ that’s what she says, and
none but a denied fool of a cornstalk what don’t
know a side-saddle from a stern-wheeler would say
dif’rent.”
“Lor’, Jim, don’t get shirty, old man,” said
Tom, who, being a Sydney-side native, was con-
temptuously regarded as a “colonial” by Killen
(imported by Government at a cost of £2O 3s 4d
in the days of assisted immigration). “ You know
better’n me, of course. But what are you goin*
to do ? You won’t let her through to-night ? It’s
after knock-off time, now.”
“ W’en a cove as don’t know a steamer from a
barge wants to boss this bridge, I tells him to shut
up straight. So now, Tom Hopwood, shut up.
The little Lizzie’s a’goin’ through if the skipper
wants her !”
And though the dusk was fast settling down
into the long, sweeping reach that was crossed by
the floating bridge, and though the steam-whistle
of the crane engine had shrieked the knock-off
signal full fifteen minutes before, Jim swung wide
the crazy old pontoons into the muddy stream.
They swayed on their hempen hinges sullenly and
oreakingly, as though, too, convinced that it was
quite time to end their . jng day’s work.
The men hauled in one rope, slacked the
bight of the other, and waited for the steamer.
Tom had shirked his pull somewhat, and Jim
knew it. But he heeded his mate’s ill-temper as
much as he did the mutual grinding of the pon-
toons, or the vexed beat of the river-ripples as
they wrestled with the crumbling bank. If the
Lizzie wanted to go through she should, and if she
didn’t, well, it didn’t matter. So Jim, surly and
precise always, and surly and precise in a double
degree when it was a case of swinging his bridges
after hours, resolved. Jim, like many a better
and many a worse man, was, as he himself ex-
pressed it, “a bit weak on the womanines. ” That’s
what he used to say when accused of favouringthe
Lizzie by letting her pass the bridge at all sorts of
unreasonable times and periods. But there was
the Victoria and the Emily Jane, and half-a-
dozen other boats with feminine appellations, and
the Alice, and he would never budge from the
strict mark of duty and routine for them. And
this circumstance—coupled with the fact that
when flour was £7O a ton at Wilcannia and
potatoes £l5, and the great Cumberocfna and the
little Lizzie, both loaded to the pilot-house with
produce, were starting at the same moment for the
drought-smitten Darling township, he had acci-
dentally jammed a pontoon against the Cum-
beroona’s starboard paddle (thereby smashing the
box and several floats) —had tended rather to dis-
credit his affected partiality for the sex. “ It
isn’t the Lizzie Jim favours,’’said “Gus” Pierce, of
the Undaunted, the satirist of Riverina. “ It’s
the Lizzie’s mate ! ” Gus was right; though it
scarcely needed his keen vision to discern as
much. Anyone with the proverbial half-an-eye
could have perceived the true cause of Mr.
Killen’s willingness to oblige the Lizzie. Cer-
tainly no one who noticed how, on this September
evening, the ruggedness of his face softened as th©
“ mate ” hailed him, could have failed to under-
stand the motive for his acts of favouritism. For,
sure enough, it was the Lizzie and her consort, the
Wombat, that with great animalish gaspings and
pantings stemmed the strong river, and forged
breathlessly round the willowy bend, through
the pontoons, past the half-buried bull of
Cadell’s old Lady Augusta, to her berth
under the wharf - crane-«. “ Thank you,
Jim,” shouted the “mate” from the pilot-
house, “ you’re a good soul to let me through to-
night. It’s worth ten pounds to me.” And a
flattered smile ironed out the furrows in Jim’s
countenance, as he waved his hand in acknow-
ledgment of the words. High-pitched was the
majestic voice, but there was a note of mournful
music in it which Jim, with his rude fancy, com-
pared to a curlew’s cry. No man's voice could
claim that trill, surely. The mate of the Lizzie
was a woman.
Chapter 11.
There had been the very devil to pay on the rivers
when Capt’n Ringsley—who, after running one
season under M'Culloch’s flag, and another under
Whyte, Counsel’s - had,inthethird year, purchased
the little Lizzie with her barge Wombat, and put
his wife to the wheel. It was done as a joke at first,
the mates and deck-hands thought, and they
entered into the spirit of the freak. They cheered
the little woman as she piloted the craft in and
out of shoaling channels, by snaggy gullets, and
over treacherous reefs. And they “ put their
money on ” her and the Lizzie when it came to
racing the big Princess or the stately Cumberoona,
for what the more powerful boat gained in steam-
ing, the little ’un more than won back by the deft
way she was handled. And they worked with
new vigour when wrought upon by the kindly
criticism of her glance.
When, however, in the beginning of Capt’n
Kingsley’s fourth river season it was made
apparent that he was shipping no mate, but that
his pretty, youthful wife, a mere slip of a girl she
was then, was going to take turn and turn with her
husband, the skipper, there was open rebellion on
the part of the Lizzie’s crew, and deadly anta*
gonism, covert and overt, from the hands of other
boats. Men whom the event directly concerned,
men whom by the widest stretch of imagination it
could not be conceived to concern, joined in the
row. The whole river population—from Albury
to Goolwa, from the Wakool Junction to Wagga ?
and from Wentworth to Wilcannia—were in
agitation. The mate whom Mrs. Kingsley dis-
placed had almost as much to say on the matter
as botty Bill the loafer, who never had a wash
except when he was thrown into the river in a
squabble, and who never did an honest day’s work
out of gaol.
Unto Captain Richard Kingsley came Captain
Freeman, Inspector for the Melbourne Under-
writers. “You’re frightened of the insurances,
are you, Mr. Freeman?” politely inquired the
former. “ Now, my wife doesn’t get drunk. And,
tell me, how many down cargoes come to grief and
the river’s bottom through a drunken mate at the
wheel?” Captain Freeman walked away.
Unto the Captain came a deputation of skippers.
“Look here, gentlemen,” he said, “ I’ve a little
girl down in Melbourne. She can’t live with us
on the river or in Echuca—the weather’d kill her.
My wife does a mate’s duty, saves a mate’s wage>
and we keep that child where she has a chance for
life. It’s my wife’s own wish-, not mine, and as I
haven’t paid for this boat and barge yet, I don’t
intend to cross her.”
Upon the heels of the skippers’ deputation there
came a number of deck-hands. “Men,” said
Kingsley, “I’m skipper of my own boat !*’ They
persisted. “Men,” he said, “If you don’t like
my service, clear.” And they did so. Not that
they were afraid of trusting themselves to the
“ mate’s ” skill, but the idea of being bossed by a
woman galled their manhood. And as no other
men would till the revolters’billets, Kingsley filled
up the vacancies with Chinese. He could not
foresee that in enlisting nine or ten odorous Ah
Fats and Ah Leans and Moy Sins on his wages-
sheet he was placing the axe to the roots of his
life and his fortunes.
They served him and the missic-matie admir-
ably. Whatever the Pagan was put regularly to
do, he did with that unerring instinct of imitation
in which consists his intelligence. As deck-hand
and rouseabout, as stoker, as cook and steward,
as lumper of wool-bales on the river-banks, as
outter-up of firewood and stower of cargo, John
was active and untiring. But where initiative
and readiness of wit were required he was almost
as miserable a failure as a new-chum who berthed
on a river-boat to earn tucker “till his remittances
arrived.”
Captain Dick stuck a sounding-pole in Ah
Ling’s hands one afternoon on an up-river trip.
“ Ah Ling, you put stick in water, and when
water comes to this mark you call “By the mark
—three. And to this mark —four, and to this
mark —five, and to this mark—six. You savee?”
After half-an-hour’s patient instruction, Ah
Ling “ saveed,” and the skipper relieved his wife
at the wheel. They were in a long, deep reach
where the ten-foot pole should not touch bottom.
Dick thought he’d test his pupil.
“Sound, Ah Ling,” shouted the Captain.
“ Sound!”
With a coolness that indicated a mastery of his
business, the Chow dropped in the pole and
boldly sent forth the awe-inspiring cry of “ By-
y’-mark, Fwee!”
“Three! Heaven!” There was no shallow
ridge, or bottom thereabouts, but a great snag
must have shifted into the boat’s course—and the
Wombat was down with produce seven feet ! In
a second the skipper had his wheel hard down,
and the engine-room telegraph had been signalled
“ full speed astern,” and the bargeman shriekingly
ordered to “stop ’way.” Dick recovered from
the fear of seeing his crafts snagged in mid-stream
just in time to hear Ah Ling repeat the last
words of his lesson—“By-y’-mark, six”—and to
hear his mate, Nell, who had rushed forward,
laugh with a laugh that was a half-sob —“Go
ahead, Dick, it’s over the pole. This gaudy old
parrot doesn’t know any better !”
Another day Andy M‘Bean, the Lizzie's en-
gineer and the only white man who had remained
on board after the Chinese invasion told his
stoker, Sun Leo, to watch the steam-gauge, while
he, Mac, made up, by a short nap, for the loss of
sleep during the previous night when they had
been running till the small hours. “Water get
down here, Sun,” said Mac, pointing to a safe
point on the tube, “ you come and shako me !”
Mac coiled himself in the shade of his wood-pile
and slept the sleep of the just man and the tired-
out engineer for a couple of hours. Then he was
awoke by the hiss and the bite of scalding steam.
Sun Lee had been relieved on the night watch, but
if his boss slept in the day time why shouldn’t he?
So he allowed his silky eyelids to droop over the
almond orifices through which his soul looked out
on the world, and he. too, slumbered sweetly
while the steamer plodded along the Twenty-mile
Reach, and the water in the boiler evaporated,
and the tell-tale in the gauge got lower and lower.
Sun Lee and his brother heathens were nearer
Heaven at the moment Mac woke up than they
were ever likely to be again, unless some other
steamer’s boiler blew up. “ The closest shave I
ever knew,” breathed Andy to himself, as he
flung open valves and cocks with trembling fingers,
which did. not lose their tremulousness till they
had encircled Sun Lee’s dreamy eyes with grimy
rings of puffy flesh.
A close shave indeed ! So close a shave that
the skipper and mate henceforth had to do double
duty, because they could not trust any single
Pagan further than they could see him. For the
mechanical routine which an educated donkey
would have gone through with ease John was
invaluable ; but as to all else he wasn’t worth his
ration of rice, Kingsley would have thrown the
individual and collective Chinese overboard but
for the “missie matie.”
Chapter 111.
She stuck to the Chows when Kingsley’s better
judgment would have sent them adrift.
“ Dick, there’s Katie, and, dear, I don’t like to
be beaten. I’ll work double, but go back to the
old plan I won’t. I’ll not confess we’re beaten.
We save forty pounds a month by having them
aboard. That’ll buy us a new barge next season.
And, Dick, how the rivers ’ud laugh at us having
to own up we’re wrong.”
So six-foot Dick, with the broad shoulders and
the bronzed face, was wheedled by his fatherly
love and his wife’s cooing and his skipper’s pride
into continuing a very foolish policy. He was
manly enough to face a crowd of drunken deck-
hands when they rushed from the wharf-side
on to the Lizzie’s deck to avenge the insults to
their class implied in the engagement of the
Chinese; manly enough to face them and strong
enough to thrash them. Nevertheless, his love
and his pride made him weak, and he kept to his
plan. He worked double-tides himself; was
skipper, deck-hand, mate, and bargeman in turns.
And worked his wife, too, double-tides. The
rougher work she could not, of course, manage;
but her little hands grasped the wheel almost con-
tinuously while steam was up. She would stand
in the pilot-house for nine and ten-hour watches,
clad in neat blue serge with bright brass buttons.
Beat the sun ever so hotly, or blew the wind
ever so blastingly, she kept her post. In her
long spells of duty she learnt the river so
thoroughly that no other mate possessed an equal
knowledge. She knew all the landmarks and
guiding points, and where the water shoaled, and
where the currents would lodge the snags. She
threaded with delicacy of touch the boat’s way
under overhanging gums to dangerous landing-
places, and up shallow channels to wood piles, and
when she was at the wheel the boat seemed to steal
from her something of womanly felicity of move-
ment, and went in and out of the pinches and
bends with a more graceful swaying of her stem
and a softer beat of her paddles. By the close of
Kingsley’s fourth season, his mate Nell was the
most accomplished steering-hand, bar Bill Davies
and Ted Barnes, on the three rivers. Even the ri yer-
men who hated Kingsley for his alliance with the
Celestials admitted as much. When six steamers
and nine barges were stuck up by the falling water
at Campbell’s Island, Kingsley’s mate put the nose
of the Lizzie straight up the channel, and with
swift, nervous balancing of the wheel, drove her
through devious cross-cuts and over treacherous
spits of sand that masked ghastly snags beneath
their glittering whiteness into deep water.
“ Damn’d if she war’t take th’ craft over wet
grass nex’! ” cried the Riverina’s mate, Jim
Morris, with an honest chord of admiration ring-
ing in his rough voice.
And if you think anybody on the rivers desired
greater praise than that, you don’t know the
Murray boating trade !
* # *
The close of that fourth season brought
them, though Capt’n and Mate Kingsley knew it
not, the beginning of the end. Fate had dealt
out to them some first-class cards, but she had
made the shuffle with her hand covered with a
poisoned glove.
During the summer off-season when the rivers
were down, and the boats laid up, the Kingsleys
visited Melbourne to taste the rare joys of a sweet
home life for a few months. After one happy day
spent on the St. Kilda Beach beneath ti-tree
clumps, in and out of which the father and mother
played hide-and-seek with their laughing child,
the Capt’n strolled into the baths. He rolled
himself luxuriously in the pungent waves as was
his wont in the greenish Murrumbidgee after a
warm day’s work on Hay Wharf ; and shewed the
other bathers a trick or two of fancy swimming.
But they looked strangely at him as he leapt out
of the water, with a curious shrinking feeling
made visible in their glance. For upon his head
and limbs and body, like miniature moons sur-
rounded by rose-flushed halos, were white spots
encircled with pinkish aureoles. All over him,
from the broad forehead that he had held aloft as
a frontlet of manly pride in the face of all men,
to the curving ankle which it would have defied
the skill of Phidias to mould in marble, the horrid
blotches appeared.
He saw the things himself. As he gazed upon
them, scarcely alarmed as yet, and with no
dawning of the awful truth glimmering its
way into his brain, his strength went from him—
the strength which had nurtured the copious
hairiness of the massive chest whereon the spots
clustered most closely—and he fell, a log, on the
deck of the bathing platform.
One only of the bathers drew near him - a young
doctor. “ Keep back all ! ” the latter cried,
and then ordered blankets to be brought. With
careful touch he covered Kingsley up, and then,
striving vainly to overcome a natural repugnance,
whispered to the barely conscious man, “ Where
do you live ?” Dick told him, muttering with a
feeble discordance, and prayed : “ What is—the
matter with me?”
The doctor rose up, and motioned the staring
bather’s and attendants away. They obeyed the
gesture—some who were half-attired gathering up
their garments, and dressing as they went.
The doctor knelt again by Dick’s side.
“ Are you a man ?” he said. “Canyou take—a
blow ?”
“ Yes,” answered Dick, “ if you—don’t hit—my
wife—and child—as well.”
“ The blow will hit all you care for.”
He paused, and the air was heavy with the
respirations of the two men before lie went on.
“ You are a white leper—and a dead man !”
Chapter IV.
The old order of things partially resumed its
sway on the steamer Lizzie and her consort, the
barge Wombat, with the beginning of the
Kingsley’s fifth season. A white-man crew
superseded the spawn of the lazar-house. But
Jim Morris, late of the Riverina, was skipper,
while Matie Nell retained her post. The rivers
did not object to that arrangement when they
were told that Capt’n Dick had been smitten by
mortal sickness and would never finger wheel-
spokes again. Upon the news, the great heart
of the river manhood gave one sharp, short beat
of gladness, and then another of shame at itself,
•and then went on pulsing regularly in sympathy
with the little woman.
The rivers did not know how awful was the
doom that had overtaken their erstwhile enemy.
Nebulous popular belief shaped itself at last into
the notion that Dick’s masterful brain w*»s

I mean—Are you the Cove ? ”
“ Yes, I’m the Cove,” the Squatter said ;
The Swagman answered, “ Right,
I thought as much ; show me some place
Where I can doss to-night.”

thrown out of gear by a sunstroke; and it was
good 0r Nell that nothing of the ghastly
truth penetrated to the quick intelligence of the
boatmen. Deck-hands would have fled the Lizzie
as a thing accursed, and stevedores would have
refused to handle freight. For the river men
would have concluded—and justly—that had
Capt n Dick kept himself from contact with the
leprous brood the hideous canker would not have
rotted his splendid virility. When a white man
clutched poles, and ropes, and fenders, after
Chinese paws had slimed them over, what could
he expect but that some of the slime should stick ?
That is what they would have said had they
known. Fortunately, they knew next to nothing.
Fate was so far merciful.
Only, of all Riverina, did Jim Killen the punt-
man know. A worthless son of Jim’s had died in
Wbntworth Hospital while the Lizzie lay at the
wharf at the junction township. Nell had nursed
thb scamp in his last hours with sisterly tender-
ness. Thereupon Jim had raved that what he
could do for the Mate he would do,
“The Mate, she loosed my Ned’s warp-lines
gently, an’ he hadn’t to let go with a thud,” he
had said when he spoke of the incident.
Up to the coming upon them of Kingsley’s doom
Killen had done no more than throw an occasional
ten-pound note into the Captain’s way by dropping
the- Lizzie through the pontoons after hours, so
that the boat might have first chance with a dis-
engaged crane. But in the hour of her extremity
Mate Nell thought of the puntman. Dick craved
to return to the river-side; he would live in a bark
humpy or a tent, he said, anywhere, in anything,
so long as it was within sight of a river and
within the sound of a steamer’s whistle and ex-
haust, and where he could sometimes see his wife.
And Nell took the puntman into her confidence.
He helped her. “So Capt’n Dick’s a-goin’ with
the stream, is he?” he said. “I never liked that
Chow bus’ness, but I’ll help you for Ned’s sake,
Mrs. Kingsley. You let Ned’s warps drop quietly
into the current, ma’am, an’ the Capt’n shan’t go
with a rush, if I can help it.”
He built a rough humpy on a thickly-timbered
twenty-acre block he had purchased on the Moama
side, and there Dick was brought by night-stages
from Melbourne. In the late autumn this hap-
pened, and with the first winter rains the rivers
were “ up.”
The season promised to be a long one. The
Hay, Balranald, and Wentworth wharves still
were burdened with back-block wool left over
from last season. Every station bn the river and
out back was crying out for produce, and stores,
and sawn timber, and the late wool would give
return cargoes till the new clip was rushed down.
These bright prospects made Dick desperate!
He moaned in his gunyah tomb, and fretted his
hfiart away quicker than the scaly patches ate
into his flesh. The next seven months should
have given him fortune, and instead he was being
offered death!
•Every sound of nature or of man pained him
with a sharper than physical pain. The steam-
saws at Macintosh’s mills on the opposite bank
tore him with their teeth ; and again and again he
suffered the throes of dissolution as he pictured
himself being crushed between the gigantic logs
that fed their steel maws. That was all he was fit
for—to throw himself beneath the logs. Other
men could carve the timber into shiny, golden
sovereigns, each of which would melt into some
joy or buy some happiness—but for him, the only
timber he needed was six pine planks for a shell 1
Then the laughing-jackass would stop in his
work of snake-hunting to cast a sneer at him from
the crown of unstripped gums, and the young
cioadae would join in the taunting chorus from the
teeming grass. And he would deride himself for
being their revengeless sport.
Then the rippling stream, on which he dared not
look in day-time save between the interstices of
thick foliage, stung him with its jesting whispers.
It asked hiin why he did not drag money from its
bosom, and when he did not answer itself answered
for him. It flaunted an iridescent bubble on its
surface that momentarily mirrored his dreadful
features.
So with everything. The monotone of the
curlew was the wail of his despair, the steam-
whistle spoke to him defiantly and dared him
ever to disturb the forest glades with its shriek
again; the fluff of the wattle showered upon him
the jingle of the gold whose hue it had stolen.
Ihe distant hoarseness of the men as they called
the soundings and the screech of plumaged parrots
made him shiver, for they reminded him of Ah
Ling and he knew now the significance of the
tiny spot on Ah Ling’s neck! And the sky,
whether it glared in brassy brilliancy or opened
its storm-fountains, tortured him, for the one
aspect told him the hot sun would draw down the
mountain snows, and the other bade him reflect
how the swollen tributaries would pour their
wealth into the main streams and give the boats
another month of activity when every hour would
be worth a yellow coin. Life mocked him at
every turn.
So, in effect, he saw on the slate, which, his sole
means of communication with the living world in
his wife’s absence with the boat, he was accus-
tomed to leave on the border of his woody prison •
and Jim Killen, reading the chalked words with
laborious eyes, muttered, “ He’s a-goin’ looney.”
And when, in the September night on which we
saw Jim first, he waited at the head of the cutting
after throwing the bridge across the stream, for
Mrs. Kingsley in order to escort her through the
darkness to the leper’s home he recalled the words
so that he might break to her the frenzy of her
husband.
“ What news, Mr. Killen?” she asked, shaking
his hand. 8
Bad, Im afraid, ma’am. He’s loosing his
lines in a way I don’t like. He’s a-goin’ looney.”
“Oh, God!” She could say no more, but fell,
trembling, against a wattle that, shaken by the
pressure, dropped a golden snow on her head.
Oh God ! That is too much !”
He put on the slate two days ago, as near as I
remember, these words :
h&ve cursed God, And yet He lets me live.
T. very snakes laugh at me when I want them to
bite me, I took a tiger-snake to bed with me last
night, and lam alive still. Say the word, Nell
and I will kill myself. I won’t do it till then
because I promised you. But say the word
darling. ” ’
“Oh, Mr. Killen, Oh, Jim, what shall I do’”
wept Mate Nell.
Jim knit his brows painfully. “You won’t be
vexed, ma’am ?” he muttered, at last.
“Vexed, Jim, my friend! How could Ibe
vexed ?”
“Well, I’d say the word, ma’am, It ’ud be
most merciful to you, an’ him, an’ the little
daughter !”
“Never, Jim, never ! Better be a leper’s wife
than the widow of a suicide’!”
“ It’ll be that in any case, ma’am. But if you
"•;>n t say the word you’ll just have to stop with
him.'
“He won’t allow me !” moaned Mate Nell. “ I
did not want to start this season—l wished to
stop with him. But he said we would be.robbed
if I did not go, and no one could watch the rivers
as I would!”
To watch the rivers was necessary to play the
game of speculation successfully. An exact cal-
culation which would get a boat and barge loaded
with produce down the “ summer-level ” Murray
in time to meet the first freshets of tne Murrum-
bidgee or Darling, might win a couple of thousand
pounds, while an error of a few hours would entail
the loss of hundreds. And it took the owner’s eye
always to note the rise and fall of the stream, so
Dick thought.
They moved on—-the man wondering how it
was all going to end ; the woman stupefied and as
in a dream, the dilated pupils of her eyes alone
showing she was awake to the horror that awaited
her.
Chapter Y.
The next down-stream trip from Echuca, Mate
Nell was mate of the Lizzie no longer. She was
“bargeman ” of the Lizzie’s barge, Wombat, and
the old bargeman had taken her place on the
steamer. And aft on the barge, in a newly-built
compartment, went—a passenger.
Nell wouldn’t say the word which would have
released Dick from his fate, and he wouldn’t let
her remain with him. To leave him alone was to
leave him to madness. So in the dead of the
night, before the Lizzie next cleared the Customs
for Hay, a tottering spectre, sheeted in greyish
cloths, stole from out of the shadows down by the
path through George Air's ship-yard on to the
punt, and thence to the Wombat’s cabin.
* # *
The men wondered at first that they had
not seen Oapt’n Dick come aboard, but their
delicacy forbade a syllable of their surprise to
reach the ears of Mate Nell. And then Killen
dropped a hint here and there in mysterious
accents how he had seen Capt’n Dick, “An’ he’d
take his oath Dick was a gone looney.”
Down to Hay, and thence to stations beyond
for late wool; up to Echuca again ; up to Tocum-
wal for a short timber-trip and back to the port
once more to fill up with stores and wire for which
the big Bidgee stations were ravenous, went the
Lizzie and the Wombat. Another quick run would
have followed with the first-clipping of the new
wool, and back again to race the Victoria for the
Burrabogie and Hunthawang clips. First come
first to load, was the rule of the two crack
stations, and their freight carried an extra pound
per ton of “greasy,” and thirty-shillings for
* washed,” for the balance of the Lusitania’s cargo-
room was booked for their wool, and, filled or not
the ocean freight would have to be paid. Up to
Burrabogie, beating M‘Oulloch’s boat by half-a-
day, and back to Hay, there to top up with
Hunthawang bales, and thence home for Christ-
mas. This was the plan.
Golden trips in golden weather, all these pas-
sa&es ! Every hundred revolutions of the Lizzie’s
paddles minted a sovereign as the Leper calculated
on his slate. If the water would hold up till
January or February they could then sell the
crafts, and, with the proceeds added to the
season’s earnings, bid the rivers farewell. They
would seek thereafter some distant home-and
wait calmly for the end! This was Capt’n
Dick s notion, for once back on the river, the
swish of the floats as they churned the water,’ and
the throbbings of the pistons, imparted something
of their restless vigour to his enfeebled system,
and thoughts were struck out of him as masterful
and bold as in the old days.
“We shall have a happy Christmas, after all,
■Nell,” he wrote on his slate, and passed it through
the partition to his wife’s hands, just before they
left Pollard’s Wharf, at Hay, on the last trip of
the year.
“ grant it, dear !” she whispered, as she
went out to trim her barge.
The wharf-loungers cheered the little woman as
the barge drew by, and wished her the best of
good luck, and the happiest of Christmases, and
the speediest of recoveries to the old man. Por a
moment, the cloud lifted, and against hope she
hoped and believed their hearty wishes would come
true. As Skipper Jim sounded the last whistle
she turned and kissed her finger-tips to the throng.
, became a tradition, that kiss. The glory of the
river-trade has departed, but the memory of Mate
iSells farewell lingers yet in nooks and corners of
the .Riverine country.
Por Hay never welcomed Mate Nell again.
* # *
The craft had reached Canoon when the
water fell suddenly. That very morn the sun had
risen hery red, and as he ran his course he trailed
n * urtl scorc hing blasts and steaming mists
that wanted only a solitary spark to link the
heavens to earth in a chain of flame.
And the spark fell!
, n .the great reach, 15 miles below Canoon,
Morris found he had to run a gauntlet of fire.
Magnificent eucalypti bordered the sorrowing
stream with spires of flame, and the tangled under-
growth spread the lurid contagion from clump to
clump with an unquenchable rapidity. Por miles
in their front, miles on either side, and miles to
their rear, the torrent of fire rolled on, roasting
boat-hands with its heat, barring return with
mammoth trunks that fell hissing into the stream
and threatening to stop their egress from the fur-
nace with like impediments.
Every eye on the steamer was strained with a
forward gaze, and none noticed that the piles of
wool on the barge ignited. The bales had not
been tarpaulined, and a fiery shower from a
thicket of ti-tree had set the packs ablaze. Nell
was the first to see the fire from the barge-bridge
where she held the wheel, and she cried for help. ’
Some of the steamer-hands rushed to save her
at the moment, the Wombat reeled and
shuddered as she struck bottom. Drawing two
feet more than the steamer, she was aground.
Morris put full towage way on the Lizzie. The
paddles lashed the cindery current into foam, and
the great shaft seemed as though it would burst
from its bearings with its Titanic strokes. But this
effort had the most fatal of results, for it severed
the tow-rope. As a bird freed from a cage, the
Lizzie sprang forward and left the Wombat
aground and wrapped in her cerements of smoke
and flame.
There was no returning. The shoaling water
threatened the Lizzie with the same fate if she
delayed her advance. Morris lingered till death
had nearly completed his leaguer around him and
his men also, and then, then, went ou—leaving
Captain Dick and his Mate Nell on their funeral-
pyre.
Perhaps it was fancy, perhaps not, but Morris
looking back, thought he saw a woman’s hand
eofc itself from the smoke as if waving a k'ss
‘ hor little Kate,” Morris whispered. “Por
little Kate! ”
