Measuring the Baby.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
We propped our crowing cherub
Against the parlour wall,
A dado topped the wainscot,
And the boy was just as'tall—
A pink and yellow dado
Of fashion quaint and new,
Relieved by pea-green roses
And buttercups of blue.
We measured that noisy infant—
Our latest young re-ruit—
With fond, parental chuckles,
For a tiny sailor suit.
And the greasy fists of Bobbie
Fell fast on mamma’s nose,
As she took his dear dimensions
And kissed his restless toes.
His eyes were bright as diamonds—
His lips were damp and red —
A few weak flaxen tresses
Were sticking to his head ;
And we said, ’twixt sighs of rapture,
Like this life’ll not remain—
When Spring brings round her jonquils
We’ll measure our pet again.
Ah me ! with a smile of sorrow,
And a laugh of deepest woe,
We have measured the sturdy baby
We breeched six months ag >;
And the bandy legs, then dimpled,
And the mottled arms so strong,
And the little trunk of Robert
Are twice as fat and long.
That pink and yellow dado,
Once level with his head,
Now reaches past the middle
Of our darling’s back, instead ;
And the clothes his mother makes him,
Although they bag at first,
Scarce last our growing treasure
A month before they burst.
We measured the costly baby
With ribbons snowy white,
Marked off in eighths of inches,
For another suit to-night;
And out of the dadoed parlor
We rushed with a stifled sob :
■ v y o,l ld that the distant angels
Might come and call for Bob !
EDMUND FISHER.
The Cruise of the In Memoriam;
Or The Ship That Came Back.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
The wan light of a stormy dawn
Lit up a tossing ship ;
It was the In Memoriam
Upon a mourning trip.
Wild waves were on the windward bow,
And breakers on the lee ;
And through her sides the women heard
The seething of the sea.
“ O, Captain !” cried a fair widhw,
Her white hands olaspfed she,
“ Thinkst thou, if drown’d in this dread storm,
That saved we shall be ?”
“You speak in riddles, Lady dear,
How savfed can we be
If we are drown’d?” “Alas, I mean
In Paradise !” said she.
“ I’ve sailed North, I’ve sailed South ”
(He was a godless wight),
“ But, boy or man, since my days began
That shore I ne’er did sight!”
The Captain told the First Mate bold
What the fair lady said ;
The First Mate sneered in Ins black beard—
Quoth he, “I am damned !”
“ Full forty souls are here aboard,
A-sailing on the wave—
Without the crew, and, ’twixt us two,
I think they've none to save—
“ Full forty souls, and each one is
A widower, or widhw,
They weep the scuppers full ; the ship
Is waterlogged with woe.”
Again he sneered in his black beard,
“ The cruise is not so brief,
But, ere we land on earthly strand
All will have found relief. ”
“ Nay, nay,” the Captain said, “ First Mate,
You have forgotten one
With eyes of blue; the tears are true
From those dear eyes that run !
“ She mourns her sweetheart drowned last year,
A seaman he, forsooth !
I would not drown for Christ his crown
If she were mine, Fair Ruth !”
“ Brave words ! but words,” the First Mate cried,
“ Are wind ! Behold in me
The warmest lover and the last!
Mine shall the maiden be.”
. . • • • • • '
Fair Ruth stood by the taffrail high,
A cross dropped in the sea,
“ If you lie here, my sweetheart dear,
By this remember me !”
Fair Ruth stood by the taffrail high,
A ring dropped in the sea,
“ Marry him not, ye false mermaids,
For married he’s now to me !”
The heav’ns flashed flame ; a black cloud came.
Its wings the sky did span,
And hover’d above the fated ship
Like death o’er a dying man.
Bended the spars, and shrieked the shrouds,
The sails flew from the mast,
And, like a soul by fiends pursued,
The ship fled through the blast.
“ More sail! more sail!” the First Mate cried
(The Captain stood aghast),
“ More sail! more sail!” and he laughed in scorn,
All by the mizen mast.
“ O, brethren, dear, there's nought to fear,
The steward told me so !”
’Twas the parson meek who thus did speak,
Just come up from below :
“And were there,’ he said, with upraised head,
And hands clasped piously,
“ I have a sainted spouse in Heav’n—
I trow she waits for me. ”
Then grimly laughed the false First Mate,
“ Good parson, let her be !
I’ve a wife in every port, but that —
And that we shall not see.”
“ Oli pardon seek ! ’ cried the parson meek,
“ And pray, if pray you can,
For much I fear, by your scornful sneer,
That you are a sinful man.”
Then louder laughed the false First Mate,
Louder and louder still,
And the wicked crew laughed loudly too,
As wicked seamen will.
“ O, Captain !” whispered a gentle dame,
“ When shall we see the land !”
The Captain answer’d never a word,
But clasped her by the hand.
“Come Heav’n, come Hell,” cried the fair widbw.
“ For me—l have no fear,
I pray ye let me land where I
May meet my husband dear.”
The Captain turned him round and said—
“ If this wind holds to blow
Ye’ll meet in either place ere night,
But which—the saints may know,,”
“My love’s in Heav’n,” quoth the fair widow,
“For, when he passed from view,
In golden script on a marble crypt
They carved it fair and true.”
• •
Day after day, night after night,
On, on the ship did reel;
The Captain drank with the second mate,
The First Mate held the wheel.
Down came the black cloud on the ship,
And wrapped her like a pall,
And horror of awful darkness fell
Upon them one and all.
The night had swallowed them utterly,
None could his fellow see,
But ghostly voices up and down
Went whisp’ring fearsomely.
No faint ray shone from moon or sun,
The light of Heav’n was gone,
But ever the First Mate held the wheel,
And ever the ship rushed on.
. . . • • •
Fair Ruth knelt down in that grim gloom,
She prayed beneath her breath :
“ God carry me o’er this dread sea
That seems the Sea of Death !”
She ceased and, 10, a lurid glow
O’er that dark water spread,
And in the blackness burned, afar,
A line of bloody red.
“ What lights are yon?” the Captain said ;
The First Mate answered then ;
“ No lights that ever shone upon
The world of living men.”
“ Down on your knees !”the parson cried,
“ Thank God, for all is well!”
The First Mato sneered ; “Those lights, they are
The Harbour lights of Hell. ”
On flew the ship to ev'ry lip
An ashen pallor came,
For all might see that suddenly
The sea had turned to flame.
The lights were near ; the Sea of Fear,
Amid the silence dire,
On that dread shore broke evermore
In soundless foam of fire.
“ O, what are yon grey ghosts and wan ? ”
The parson cried, “ who seem
With coloured strings of beads to play,
As in a dreadful dream ?”
“Damned souls,” the First Mate said, “they sit
And count, through endless years,
The moments of Eternity
On beads of burning tears. ”
‘And who,” exclaimed the fair widbw,
“ Is that grim ghost I see
Who stands upon a rock, and girns
And wags his head at me ? ”
“Your husband dear,” the First Mate sneered,
“ At last you have your pray’r—
He says Hell is not Hell to him
Unless you, too, are there.”
“ Then, will you land, my fair widhw ?’•
(Full scornful were his tones) pt
“For you he aches, for you he makes
Hell weary with his moans.”
Three paces took that fair widhw,
Three paces on the deck, *“■
Then suddenly she turned and fell
Upon the Captain’s neck.
“O, Captain, dear, my mourning year
Is o’er—l would not own
That grinning ghost—while there remains
One man of flesh and bone !”
The wretched spectre heard the words,
And, in his anguish great,
He beat his head against the rooks—
Loud laughed the false First Mate.
Then “ Who are you,” the parson said,
“ That talks so free of Hell ?”
“ My name is Satan,” he replied,
“ Have I not steered you well?”
“ Back—back the yards !” the Captain cried ;
Then quoth the false First Mate :
“ Like many more who sight this shore—
You back your yards too late.”
“ There are the dear deceased you mourned J
With such exceeding zest, ,/ i:
They call you—whoso freely goes
E’en yet may save the rest.
“ Soul-saving is the parson’s trade,
He here may save a batch.
Where is he?—speak !” The parson meek
Was safely under hatch.
One pale ghost waved the vessel back
With gestures sad and dumb —
Fair Ruth has plunged into the sea,
“ My love, my love, I come ! ”
All in a moment shone the sun,
Blue gleamed the sky and sea,
The brave old ship upon the waves
Was dancing merrily.
And merrily to sound of bells *
To her old port full soon,
The In Memoriam, that went forth
Returned the Honeymoon.
There o’er their grog sea-captains still
Her wondrous story tell,
And how her Captain backed his yards
A biscuit-throw from Hell.
V. J. D.
Woman’s Rights.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It is Woman’s Right to scrub,
And to cook our daily “ grub,”
But it’s not her right to search us when we come
home from the Club.
It is her right our bread to bake
And to fry our chop and steak,
But it’s not her right to think we should keep
sober for her sake.
It is Woman’s Right to say
Just what tve think, and she may
Do that, but musn’t think of hitting out in her
own way.
It is her right to dust and sweep,
And the house in order keep,
But it’s not her right to buy up things because she
thinks they’re cheap.
It is not her place to think,
And it’s not her right to drink,
Nor to ever want to visit a theatre or a rink.
It is Woman’s Right to know
What will please her master, so
She can bring the very liquor that will drive away
his woe.
It is Woman’s Right, you see,
To do many things, but she
Mustn’t trespass on the province of the Everlasting
He.
E. DEMPSEY.
Dear Bulletin, —I would ask you to pub-
lish in your paper the undermentioned letter
received by a friend of mine in answer to an appli-
cation made by him in rc an advertisement for a
working overseer on a large station in Gippsland,
Victoria :—
Sir, —In answer to yours I have to inform you that the
man I want as working overseer must be an experienced
and capable and able workman in draining and fencing
and spade and axe work, as well as be a good ploughman
and carter. He must be able to make drains and banks
along fences and clean out ditto, or make drains with
scoop or plough, keeping a proper level to let the water
run out of the lowest places ; must be able to put up a
post-and-rail fence or with wire equal to work done on
railways, and bo a good hand with spade and long-handle
shovel, and graft and axe and must be an able man
and excel in the work, so as to show men an example,
and must keep these men to working hours. He musk
not he too young or too old, and as I have a cottage
for him I would prefer him married, hut would take him
unmarried it a really good man at his work. Wages for
married man £55 per annum, and if he gives satisfaction
£0() and two rations and milk, vegetables, potatoes and
firewood carted. If single man, £50 pet annum, and if
satisfactory £05, or even £70, and one ration, hut J
must have references from two or three employers, foi
where you have been at work and to whom I will write,
and I must know how long you have followed this work,
as well as your age, and if you are a likely man, and very
likely it references are not sdlliciently explained 1 will
fix the wages for one year a little lower, to he fixed up if
gives satisfaction, as I do not want to be troubled with
people applying, or getting hired, if not competent, and
coming here, and have to he sent away.
The fellow in receipt of this letter intends to
have a man male to order, but as it will take a
little time ho has not signed articles. —Yours, &c,,
Conscience.


The New Pygmalion.
An Idyll of Flinders Lano.
He was a sculptor, possessed of an impossible
dream, a dream of finding amid the daughters of
Hve a woman that in her beauty should equal his
own conception. The modern method of patching
this girl’s head on to another’s neck, of selecting
good arms here, a bust there, and the rest where
one could, shocked him, and so the Cleopatra he
had dreamt of still lay in the marble, and he
wandered to Melbourne partly because of his rest-
lessness and partly because a friend had told him
’twas there if anywhere lie would find the model
he wanted. “Yonder in the fair South,” this
friend had said, “ where English blood has
wedded the sun-light—and, as yet, Fashion is not
nurse—women are as God intended them to be.”
. On® morning, ’twas at the end of his first month
in Australia, and so far he had been unsuccessful,
he sat in his studio in Flinders-lane looking at the
large block of stone he had bought out of his small
store of ready money, and wondering if his ideal
woman would ever find shape. Through all hin-
drance his soul saw her lying there, in the centre,
even now, with that glorious neck of hers rising
like a Roman column from out those shoulders
whereon kings, while empires drifted, have let
their heads linger, and the face and form of her
all quiet and passionless as she lay asleep in
oblivion’s embrace awaiting the chisel of her
awakener. Suddenly a rap at the door aroused
him from his dreams, and at his bidding a girl
entered ; there was an awkward pause, and then,
with much nervousness, she said:
“Mr. Clifford, I believe?”
Clifford bowed.
“ I hear that you are searching for a model., and
I came to ask you if I would suit your purpose.
My name is Clark—Sara Clark,”
The artist looked her up and down, and in one
big draught drank in all the lines and the curves
of her and their faultless beauty. She was
Beauty personified, and crowned as beauty should
be with the head and neck of a goddess. She
wore a dress of blue serge, a material made for
the betrayal of beautiful women, as elastic in its
hold as a man’s love, and as tender and constant
as a woman’s. The healthy mind of the maker
(the girl had made it herself) had thrown to the
wind all idea of the unnatural excrescences where-
with the modern woman improves on Nature’s
handiwork, and told the willing material, in
common gratitude to show all it dared of her.
“You will not only suit my purpose,” Clifford
said when the artist in him had absorbed its full
of her, “but you are the very woman I have
dreamt of. Will you really sit to me?”
X will sit to you,” she replied, now thoroughly
self-possessed and leaning, as she spoke, back
against the stone, “on two conditions. The first
is that I am well paid, for my people are very poor
and this appears to be my only chance of making
money honestly; and, secondly .”
“ The question of payment,” Clifford broke in,
“ need not be mentioned. I will gladly pay you
all you ask.”
. The girl nodded her thanks. “My other condi-
tion is that—that—how shall I put it ? I have
heard,” she continued, after a pause, during Avhich
Clifford wondered much what was coming “I
have heard that the reputation of an artist’s
model is not always of the best, and I must take
care ipf my good name—’tis all I have—will it bo
safe in your keeping ?”
Then from the deep hollows, at the bottom of
which hiseyes lay half hidden, flashesof angerleapt.
“ God forgive you, girl,” he said, “for placing t o
true artist on the same level as other men !”
The girl saw her mistake and her great safety,
and, promising to return that he might start work
by eleven on the morrow, left him. During that
day and night Clifford finished his roughing-in ;
and in the morning, punctual to the moment, the
girl came.
“ Here is your couch,” he said, “and you will
gather from this (pointing to the marble) how X
want you to lie. You will be half on your side,
lying restfully with one arm uncovered—thrown
behind your head, the other will lie at your side,
this piece of drapery will be thrown across you!
I think it will be sufficient, thee room is warm.”
Half an-hour after, as Clifford hung over the
girl and saw the accuracy of her death-posture,
pone of the truth of its deep origin dawned on
him, he knew nothing of women nor cared for
them, but as figures to carve from. The curves of a
breast, the rounding of a shoulder—or a brave,
graceful neck, moved him to his depths, but for
the human soul beneath he had no care, nor time
to think; love for him meant love for his art —
and all his passion went out in a search for it.
Had it been otherwise, he might—from the
very attitude of the girl—have learnt that the
death in her was not assumed. Her young life so
far had been no life at all, but merely protest—a
long, angry, deep-seated protest against the
powers that had planted her queen-like form in a
pauper’s home : the deep undercurrents and the
shallow laughters—the day-dreams of a tiny hand
in hers and a stronger one about her, that
mysterious going-out after sympathy; all these
and other like yearnings that play so great a part
in the building up and beautifying of most
women’s lives had neither part nor lot in her
making.
But the crimson curtains about the east wind’s
bed hide the coming day—the twins sleep together
—look, with the expectant dead, that way, and
while the cruel sister sleeps you’ll see young rosy
morn playing about the hills.
The girl was both—the biting blast when breath
was in her and beauteous morn when silence held
her dumb.
And so, sinless of posturing, she lay like some
white blossom on the earth, no modest snowdrop
with bent head and its “I am not worthy,” but
rather some wide-mouthed, thirsting lily drinking
admiration as its own ; across her loins a soft silk
scarf was thrown, and about her head for halo the
“ noli me tangcre ” of passionless indifference.
A month has gone, and the curious man—art-led
—is finding fast, beneath the rugged stone, the
queen he dreamt of. About the room, as thick as
snow, were broken fragments of stone—Nature’s
hiding-stuff—and all these four long weeks the
girl has, through her cold, grey eyes, watched the
coming.
The artist had been to her first a mystery, then
a study, and, last of all, an education.
His impatience and hungry eyes during the
early days, when the first rough work was done,
were as incomprehensible as his loving, tender
touch when Cleopatra looked through her
stone.
Sometimes, as wrapped in heavy rugs she sat
up to eat her meagre lunch, she would talk to him
of his art, and try to fathom it, but he would
speak but rarely and then in the strange language
of a rhapsodist.
Open-mouthed, the girl would listen while ho
told her that the figure there was the best part of
him writ in unalterable stone—his own creation
that sprang from his soul—“No, not sprang,” he
would correct himself, “but conceived one night
when the silver-sandalled moonlight crept into my
room and played about my bed, and from thence
brought forth, as all things great are, with pain and
travail. ’Twas so,” he continued, pointing to the
stone ; “the ancients told their dreams of beauty
and wrote their religions, and tired multitudes
stepped aside a moment from their labours and
grew silent and stronger in the truth of it.” Then
he would move over to his work and his love-
leaking fingers would dwell long and tenderly
about Cleopatra’s reposeful lips.
“How great it is to be a maker,” she would say.
Then he, half angrily: “I am no maker but a
valet only with some knowledge of my Masters’
secrets. ”
And so the days sped on, and the sculptor glad-
dened in his heart, and the girl softened in hers ;
for—and here’s the pity of it—what the chisel had
done for Cleopatra’s glorious form the watching of
i b had done for the model’s stolid heart—found it
and laid it bare.
In the long silences, and in the presence of a
love hitherto unknown, the coldness in her had
died away and from the warm north there had
come the whisperings of something better.
As she watched Clifford turn first to her with
the calm, cold, critical stare of a connoisseur, and
then back to his stone with paternal love, a mad
jealousy took possession of her, and it was the
all-devouring jealousy of a first love born late.
As breaks the lava through the snow-clad moun-
tain, so the torrent of her yearnings through “the
winter of her discontent.
One day, it was a hot-wind day, and the fever
of work was off him, Clifford was dismissing his
model before her time. She had just returned
from her dressing-room, and stood for some
moments unnoticed behind him, for he with bent
head and folded arms was worshipping at the altar
of his own creation. The maker of two women
stood between them, and all unasked poured upon
the cold, irresponsive stone that tempest of hot
love the other thirsted for.
Suddenly Clifford turned and faced the girl,
much of whose beauty had gone from her, in the
presence of this strange something, greater than
anything she had dreamt of, and because a new
hunger and thirst had her by the throat.
“ I shall not come again,” she said; “ you have
nearly done and can finish without me. ”
“ It is impossible—why, this hand is not nearly
finished, and my drapery is only roughed in—but
why —what do you mean by this sudden determina-
tion ? Has anything hurt or offended you ? ” then
his subtle eye caught the alterations in her. “ Has
some brute been prying into your dressing-room?
Has something frightened you? Of course you
will come again, but you must not look like that,
why your shoulders are fallen together, your cheeks
have sunk, you look hungry.”
“ 1 wonder,” the girl said at last, looking up as
she spoke—“l wonder if I look at all like you did
the first day.l,saw you ? You looked hungry, I
remember.”
“I was hungering, but not for bread—but,”
and he examined her again critically, “if you look
to-morrow at all as you do to-day I don’t know
that you need return.”
Then the girl went out; she was at first glad to
be away, in the air her breath came easier, her
love was not so oppressive out of doors, and back
to her home in that squalid street. Her father
had recovered somewhat and taken a job of bill-
sticking. About their one living-room there lay
parcels of posters referring to beer, soap, insecti-
cide, and other shrewd guesses at a people’s
wants. The room was dull and comfortless, so
was the studio she had just left; but the last had
suddenly become heaven to her, the other more
than ever hell.
Then an irresistible desire seized her to go back,
and giving her hand to her yearnings she was led
into the street again. At the corner of Collins-
street she stood a moment irresolute. All around
the brass plates of vaunting specialists peered
through the twilight. Eyes, ears, lungs, all are
cured, she thought, but broken hearts—no ! then
an aching desire to be alone seized her, and she
made for the river’s bank and saw the day go, and
the moon come to the hill-top and, throwing off
her mantle of cloud, step into her Kingdom of
Night. At her feet the calm stream slipped past.
So like her old life it was—muddy, perhaps, but
passive and passionless; and now all was
changed—a new horror hung about her as a
garment! ; T was not like hunger ; she had often
known that, and the sickening weight of it. ’Twas
not hopeless ambition ; she had felt that, too—all
her short life it had been there, in her breast
somewhere. But deeper down than either, a
hollow, empty cavern, where yearnings live, had
opened itself, and the breath of it made life cold
for her.
Then she rose and, with no definite object in
view, wandered, numb and past feeling now, back
to the city, and found herself come as a mere
automaton to the little right-of-way by the side of
the studio. Suddenly a noise within the building
, disturbed her and set her thinking—who could it
be?—not Clifford; he lived miles away. Not! —
no, it could not be her rival—she was only stone.
Lifting an empty box she placed it noiselessly
beneath the window, stepped on to it, and saw
the sculptor himself leaning over his masterpiece
He had placed three candles at Cleopatra’s head,
and moved every few minutes to see new shadows
fall and old curves with now light upon them.
Then ho took a deep breath, and, stepping back,
muttered in the words of the great singer—
Each part about her was a righteous thing.
Her mouth an almsgiving ;
The glory of her garments charity ;
The beauty of her bosom a good deed.
In the good days when God'kept sight of us,
Love lay upon her eyes,
And on that hair whereof the world takes heed ;
And all her body is more virtuous
Than souls of women fashioned otherwise.
Then the girl felt sick, and getting down from
her box went home. Knots of men at the street
corners made way for her, and even stopped their
babbling of shares to notice the vacant stare and
withered face of the young girl—a face full of
“grey old miseries.” Once at homo the incom-
plete thing threw herself upon the bed, and
staring at the ceiling saw, not in the brain but in
the aching heart of her, that the four corner-stones
of a perfect womanhood were four capacities—to
love, to be loved, to wonder and to resolve. Two
of these were hers already, the last she would get
and the second should be the child of it.
In the morning she rose and spent some time in
the framing and forgiijg of a letter that was sent
directly after breakfast to the studio. This letter
purported to come from a wealthy patron of the
arts living in East Melbourne, and requested Mr.
Clifford to favour him with a visit, hinting at a
commission that would probably follow the inter-
view.
The girl, from a stable where she had secreted
herself, saw Clifford take one last look at
Cleopatra, close the door, and start; then, as one
about to do murder she crept into the studio
locked the door and faced her rival, and the over-
flowing passion of a woman with blood in her
took possession of the stone— and they sneered at
one another; the girl could swear she saw
Cleopatra’s lips curl; it strengthened her in her
resolve, and seizing the mallet and chisel that lay
near she bared her strong arm for vengeance.
Taking her stand in front of the figure, she
looked it up and down, saw its great beauty, and
hated it the more. Suddenly the pent-up passion
of a woman’s love that, when dammed, stagnates
into cruelty, found vent in blows, and in less than
three minutes Cleopatra had gone to nothingness,
and was lying about the room in small pieces of
meaningless marble; then the girl crept back to
her hiding-place with the curse of Cain upon her.
She had scarcely hidden herself when Clifford
turned the corner. He had the hang-dog look of
a man who, for the first time in his life, had come
in contact with personal cruelty. Someone had
deliberately deceived him. “And yet,” he asked
himself, “ to whom had he ever been unkind?”
He reached the door of the studio and, for a
moment, stood with his hand upon the latch,
pausing, as a child about to plunge his teeth into
luscious fruit. One he knew who could not treat
him falsely, and a woman, too ; one there was who
would always be the same to him, for was she not
built of stone, and his own making? He opened
the door and looked, and there, in the yawning
death of everything, the light went our, black
clouds floated about him, his head grew dizzy, he
fell, and kindly unconsciousness took him to her
breast and rocked him to sleep a while.
The girl saw it all, and with it realized for the
first time the full truth of what she had done.
She had deliberately broken the stone, not so
much because the stone had broken her heart, as
in the hope that the torrent of the man’s love
might then come her way, and now the truth
flashed across her that in the destruction of Cleo-
patra she had destroyed Cleopatra’s maker. She
might have known it earlier, for were they two
not of one flesh? Was not all the life she had of
his lending? Was she not, as he himself had said,
the better partof him,and couldhelivewithout her?
On the swift feet of frightened love, the girl was
instantly at his side, and the next moment had
lifted him all limp and lifeless as he was on to the
couch whereon she had spent so many curious
hours. A sharp fragment of marble had cut his
temple, and a dark stream of blood was running
down his pallid cheek and falling upon the floor.
So realistic in the girl’s mind had become the idea
of Cleopatra’s personality, that when she saw the
blood upon the stone about her she involuntarily
swung round to the rugged remnants of the figure
to see if that, too, was bleeding.
Once assured of Clifford’s immediate safety she
hurried into the back-door of an hotel near, and
from the kindly hostess begged some brandy, and
then went back to her nursing.
In an hour or two, although still unconscious,
Clifford was well enough to be moved into a bed-
room that she had hired close by.
All that day the girl sat over him —now moisten-
ing his dry lips that muttered, now shading him
from the inquisitive sun-light, and always raining
on him the continual dew of her love and
yearnings.
With evening some consciousness came, and
came, as consciousness always does from its
holiday, like a timid girl to a new boarding-
school; half curious, half afraid—one moment
asking, with thoughtful face and wrinkled brow,
the meaning of this or that, and the next retreat-
ing into the sanctity of itself.
A hideous dread lay just in front of the girl as
silently she sat and watched the ebb and flow of
Clifford’s thinking. It was the fear that jvith his
full consciousness there would come not a recog-
nition of the new self she felt, but of the old model
she hated to remember; she had loved him before
she had destroyed her rival, but now seeing the
greatness of her crime she was desperately deter-
mined to compensate for it with an overwhelming
love and a devotion that should be all-absorbing.
But how ? Love, love for the animate had no
place in his composition—it was eaten up, she
knew, by his love for the beautiful, nevertheless
his love for beauty was intact and her beauty was
still her own. It came back when fear left her,
she would bait her hook with it, angle with her
curves, and then if only she could get him
interested in her again, she would lead him by
other methods into the now realm of her newer
womanhood. And so it came to pass that when
Clifford awoke from a long sleep he found the
light so placed that its rays fell full upon her and
his hand was held by her own against her bare
neck and shoulder, the part that she knew he loved
best.
With eyes now widely opened he looked at her
long and wonderingly, then asked where he was.
In bed ; you have bee> very ill.”
“ And where —where is Cleopatra ?”
A shudder ran through the girl. “She is in
the studio.”
A smile shone through the fog of his anxious-
ness. “I am so glad; I had a horrible dream
that she ”
The girl ran her fingers through his hair as no
one but his mother had ever done. “You must
not mind your dreams —you must get well.”
Then he turned half round, nearer the fingers that
caressed him, and found, in the untiring hand of
the girl, old associations and strange memories of
the waste wilderness of his boyhood, when a
mother’s love was the only shelter.
Then, with eyes still closed, he left the past,
came up to the present, and tried to understand
it; weighed the girl’s gentle touches in new balances
and found they meant very much to him, then he
opened his eyes and looked into her face and saw
her soul there.
“ You seem to me changed very much—aie
you ? ”
“Yes.”
“ What has changed you ? ”
“ ’Tis a long story, and you cannot hear it now,
you are not well enough.”
“ I am quite well—tell me.”
And so with the cloak of night about them she
knelt down and told him all, and somehow was not
afraid.
He did not speak until she had finished, and
then putting his hand firmly upon her head bent
it back, and looking straight into her face said :
“ Pygmalion lives still, and the gods have been
again kind to him.”
MANNINGTON CAFFYN.
The Widow’s Mourning.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ I have not long to live,”
he said
To the gentle wife quite
newly wed.
But tears did not be-
come her;
So, with a smile, she bent
her head
And kissed his brow and
whispered, “Fred,
I think you’ll last the
summer !”
“ The nights are warm,
no breezes chill
The fragrant buds or
blossoms kill,
And if, old dear, you’re
wary,
With proper care I think
you will
Stay with your little wife
until
The end of February.”
Again she kissed his brow, and, oh !
His breathing then grew faint and low,
A lifeless form was present.
Said she, “ I knew it must be so,
But as, poor boy ! he had to go,
I tried to make things pleasant.”
She left the room with streaming eyes
And saw the sun in splendour rise—
Another day adorning;
Anon she murmured ’mid her sighs—
“l wonder where a widow buys
The nicest kind of mourning !”

is more likely to be put to the test than at any other period, and the dry subject
of statistics is scarcely one that can well be chosen as an aid to digestion. We will not,
therefore, dear reader, inflict upon you any very severe tax of that nature. Suffice it to
say, that it is an interesting and surprising fact that over

WARNER’S “SAFE” REMEDIES
Have been sold in these Australasian colonies and New Zealand during the past five years,
and what is more astonishing still, is, that their sale goes on increasing; a solid proof that
they are not simply nostrums got-up for the bald purpose of making money, but, on the
contrary, that they are preparations specifically adapted to the cure of the diseases for which
they are designed and honestly placed before the public, to be by them tested as to their merits.
The verdict arrived at after a fair trial is so unmistakably in their favour, that not
a word more is necessary as a recommendation to any who are unfortunately in despair of
finding, from any other source, a cure for long-endured maladies, many of them falsely and
unnecessarily declared to be beyond human aid. But the following case, as a specimen of
many hundreds, is here given :
Messrs. H. H. WARNER and CO.
Port Wakefield, South Australia, ,17 th July, 1889.
hioiu June until the end of August, 1887, I was a severe sufferer from disease of the kidneys
and urinary Organs, so much so that it became necessary to prepare my will, in anticipation of my early decease.
During my illness I obtained advice from several of the medical profession, but instead of benefiting therefrom
grew woi se and weakei, until, finally, I was confined to my bed and despaired of recovery. Among many
distressing symptoms from which I suffered acutely were hemorrhage, palpitation of the heart, throbbing pains
in the head, paralysis of the bladder and growing weakness, ending in fainting. Referring to my diaryl find
that I commenced to take Warner’s Safe Cure on 13th August, with the happy result that on Bth September
—a period of only twenty-one days—l was enabled to attend to my business/ From that time the improve-
ment, although gradual, was continuous and decided, the fluids became natural, and the tone of the system,
which had become very low from loss of blood and exhaustion, steadily rising, until normal strength was regained!
Whilst fully the goodness of Almighty God in my restoration to health and strength, I
gladly bear testimony to Warner’s Safe Cure as being the means or agent of my recovery. You are at full
liberty to use this if you choose.
(Signed) JOHN RUMBLE, Storekeeper.

NICHOLSON’S
EAR DRUMS
Cure Deafness in all its Stages.
MELBOURNE. 115, 117, 119 Collins-st., East,

1260 Santiago del Estero Antigno.
Nicholson’s Ear Drum, of which following
is a description, is simply one of the most perfect
inventions of art for the restoration of the im-
perfections of nature. The eardrum is about fof
an inch in length, and is constructed after the mould
of the natural drum of the ear from pure gum and
finished in gold and silver. It is intended either
to fill the place of an absent eardrum or to enforce
the action of an imperfect one, and may be easily
introduced into the auditory channel, whence it
can be removed and replaced at pleasure. When
placed in position and pressed back against the
natural drum, a vacuum is produced which causes
a cupping of the natural drum, should that be
collapsed or sunken, while, in cases of perfor-
ation it replaces the imperfect natural drum of
the ear. It is impossible for one who has not
suffered from deafness to write with enthusiasm
of this wonderful invention. That admirable
quality addsits convincing testimony in the numer-
ous testimonials from sufferers of all classes.
Deaf 40 Years.
5 Johnston-street, Wellington, N.Z.
23rd April, 1889.
Dear Sir, —I have received very considerable
benefit from using your Ear Drums. I have
suffered from deafness for over forty (40) years,
the result of measles in my childhood, Latterly
my deafness has been so painful that my business
talking had to be done by writing. I was nearly
stone deaf. The Ear Drums and Lotion relieved
me in about ten days, and after one month’s
wearing I could distinctly hear a sermon preached.
In my opinion, as in my own experience, your Ear
Drums and Lotion are, using very mild language,
Invaluable Remedies. My family are astonished
at their effect on myself.—Faithfully yours,
T. GLUYAS PASCOE.

86 Ferrars Street, South Melbourne,
Melbourne, July 22nd, 1889.
Respectful Sir,—Considering that I have used
your Patent Artificial Ear Drums and Lotions
since May 15th, I feel it my duty to make known
to you that I cannot find words great enough to
express myself in their favour. Having consulted
and been a patient under, the care of five of the
most skilful aurists in England and Melbourne
within the last five years, but to no good effect, I
can without hesitation state that it has been a
blessing to me that such an invention ever came
into existence. I have been a patient in the
Liverpool (England) Eye and Ear Institution,
Myrtle-street, also in the Melbourne Eye and
Ear Institution, when they pronounced me incur-
able in both institutions. The Liverpool Insti-
tute treated me for catarrh in the throat, and the
Melbourne Institute treated me for excessive wax
in the ear. Further, should any person, aurist or
otherwise, be desirous of having any other infor-
mation, I shall consider it a pleasure to answer
any inquiry personally or by letter. Sincerely
wishing you success in your simply combined
remedy. You are at liberty to make use of this
letter to vour own approval, as facts are stubborn.
—Yours most respectfully, THOS. HOOSON.

We notice with pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is now opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, which should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt
want.*

Notice to Artistic and Literary contribiutors.
Strictly Original matter contributed to Tub Bulletin
will be paid for. The Editor will be glad to receive and
consider contributions as under : —
1. Original humorous or political matter, illustrated or
otherwise. ( Drawings must be made in clean lines
of Indian ink on white paper or Bristol-board.)
2. Unpublished anecdotes of living celebrities, or people
who ought to be celebrated.
S. Humorous poems (from eight to forty-eight lines pre-
ferred ).
Serious poems, of similar or greater length, especially
those suitable for pictorial illustration.
5. Short Australian or other stories, up to, say, 3000
words. About half that length, or even less, will
be preferred.
All Contributions must be Written Legibly, on One
Side Only of the paper, and folded, not rolled. 2 he
name and address of the writer must appear, in all
; ases, on the MS. itself, and not mereh on an accompany-
ing letter or paper. The Proprietors do not hold them-
selves responsible for Dejected Contributions; but, if
accompanied by a stamped and addressed envelope or
wrapper, every effort will be made to return them.
The Editor does not undertake to enter into any
correspondence respecting rejected Manuscripts.
The Poets’ Smoke-Concert.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
I.
Swinburne, who drew metaphors:
If you were what the beer is, •
And I the lemonade,
We’d mix in countless shandies
(Despising S. and brandies)
A drink that never wearies,
I think the best that’s made.
If you were what the beer is,
And I the lemonade.
If death were what the light is,
And life were the cigar,
Then death life’s end would kindle
Till it should wane and dwindle
(A sentiment that trite is)
And soar in smoke afar.
If death were what the light is,
And life were the cigar.
11.
Tennyson, who was hazy :
Beers, foaming beers, I know not whence they
come,
Rise in the glass land gather to a head,
And gently fizzing slow subside again,
Then cool and pleasant travel down my throat,
And so become the beers that are no more.
Ah, passing strange !—before me I perceive
Two tumblers, yet there was but one before;
And —wonderful! —two pipes. Alas, my sight
Swims round and round. Ah, well, I know the
cause:
It is, it is, the beers that are no more.
111.
Walt Whitman, who rose to order:
Hang it all, gentlemen,
Mop up your liquids quietly—hallo, there, Swin-
burne,
Mind what you’re up to,
You’ve spilt all your beer down my shirt-front and
put out my cigarro.
Here’s to you all, amigos, Byron, shut up,
Your singing would turn milk sour. Pass the
bottle.
IV.
Dobson, who trioletted:
Fill the foaming glass,
Light the cheerful weed.
Let all grave thoughts pass,
Fill the foaming glass ;
Last/man drunk’s an ass.
Drink, and then with speed
Fill the foaming glass,
Light the cheerful weed.
R.C.H.
Shiels, Victorian Minister of Railways,
lately announced his intention of thoroughly re-
forming his department, and next day a man
wrote him to ask if he could refund the sum of
two-and-sixpence, which the applicant had dropped
through the spittoon of a smoking-carriage some-
where near Brighton. Also an old lady forwarded
a peremptory demand for a lost umbrella, and a
cricket-club requested him to forward a free pass
by return post, and some thirsty travellers insisted
that better liquor should be supplied at the
refreshment-rooms else they would wreck the
Government, and some teetotallers demanded
that the supply of liquors should be stopped else
they would also wreck the Government, and six
other deputations demanded 847 miles of new line,
and nearly burst with rage because they didn’t
get it.
Charles Marvin, the well-known traveller
and writer on Asiatic affairs, whose death “ at
the fatal age of 37” has just been cabled, was
once a nine days| wonder. Employed as a writer
at lOd per hour in the British Foreign Office, he
was deputed, by a well-paid official too lazy to earn
a vulgar salary, to copy out the famous Salisbury-
Schouvaloff agreement made prior to the Berlin
Treaty. Contributing to the London Globe to
eke out an existence, Marvin disclosed to that
journal the contents of the document, which,
when published, sent ahum through Europe. The
poor “ tenpenny ” was arrested and charged with
stealing an official paper, but as it had never
left the pigeon-hole at Downing-street, the case fell
through. ■

crushed 6471 tons of stone which yielded 19,506oz.
of gold.
THE GREAT REMEDY FOR PAIN.
ST. JACOBS OIL.
WHAT IT IS.
SIX STANDARD VIRTUES.
Ist. It is in one word a cure ; it is not merely a
relief, and in no sense a cure-all; it is the product
of scientific research.
2nd. It strengthens while it soothes and sub-
dues, heals, and cures ; it literally conquers pain.
3rd. Its effects are curative and permanent to
the whole group of muscular miseries and nervous
agonies.
4th. It does not merely irritate the outer
surface, nor does it merely soften or relax a con-
stricted muscle. To its specific action a superior
curative virtue is superadded.
sth. It penetrates deeply but gently; search-
ingly and surely seeking the pain spot in an effort
to conquer,
6th. Each constituent of the formula has a
recognised intrinsic virtue to serve most surely
the cure of pain.
SOME AUSTRALIAN EVIDENCE.
The editor of that favorably known and pro-
gressive periodical, the Illustrated Sydney
News, writes that he “has much pleasure in
giving the proprietors of St. Jacobs oil his testi-
mony with respect to its wonderful curative
properties. The editor has suffered acutely with
chronic sciatica, and has found the greatest relief
by the application of St. Jacobs oil to the affected
parts, and has no hesitation in recommending it
to sufferers.”
The lady who edits the Band op Mercy
(Sydney), also states that she “can testify to the
value of St. Jacobs oil, as it is used in her family
with great benefit.”

CADBURY’S COCOA is closely allied to
milk in the large proportion of flesh-forming and
strength-sustaining elements that it contains. It is pre-
pared on the principle of excluding the superabundance
of fatty indigestible matter with which Cocoa abounds
—supplying a refined thin infusion of absolutely pure
Cocoa, exhilarating and refreshing, for Breakfast, Lun-
cheon, Tea or Supper, giving staying power and im-
parting new life and vigour to growing children, and
those of delicate constitutions.
“Breakers Ahead!”
That cry has brought terror to many a stout
heart. A shipwreck is a frightful experience, to
hear the wind whistle, the timber creak and crash,
the cordage strain, the sailors swear, and the
women scream, tremble, and run hither and
thither like maniacs, is enough to appal the
bravest heart. Yet, with all its terrors, death by
shipwreck is not half so terrible as the slow and
insidious course of disease, which for the latter
years of the stricken one’s life makes existence a
misery, not only to the victim but to all around
him. With such terrible facts staring us in the
face how careful we should be to preserve that
blessing of Providence —health and strength. Note
should be taken of the first signs of disease.
A simple headache is an infallible sign that
the liver and stomach are disordered ; fre-
quent toothache and neuralgia indicate infalli-
bly that the nervous system is becoming
enervated; that tired feeling is proof positive
that strength is failing. There are hundreds of
such symptoms which should have immediate
treatment. Clements Tonic fortifies the blood and
builds up nerve tissue ; it is the greatest regulator
the system ever introduced, and thousands of
grateful sufferers testify to this fact. For in-
stance, Captain H. Dibden, Master Balmain
Ferry Boat, was for years an acute sufferer from
nervousness, debility and chronic dyspepsia. He
had recourse to Clements Tonic, which effectually
cured him. He says :—Balmain. —Dear Sir, —I
have much pleasure in giving my evidence in sup-
port of the value of Clemeuts Tonic. For a long
time I suffered from indigestion and debility, and
tried many remedies with but little benefit.
Seeing Clements Tonic advertised, I tried it, and
after the first bottle I felt quite a change, and am
now anew man altogether, and shall be glad to
afford anyone further information if desired. And
Mrs. Woodlands, Port Macquarie, suffered from
Sluggish Liver, Hsepatitis and Dyspepsia.
She took three bottles Clements Tonic and
the pain was completely gone and perfect
health restored. She writes : —Port Macquarie,
N.S.W.—Dear Sir, —For many years I have been
suffering from very severe pain in the side and
indigestion. I tried all sorts of remedies and
consulted many medical men, but got no relief. I
tried Clements Tonic, and before I had taken three
bottles the pain in my side was completely gone,
and I never felt so well in my life before. And
Mr. S. D. Wood, Yerger of All Saints Cathedral,
Bathurst, and a most reputable and respected
citizen of “the city of the plains,” frankly en-
dorses this wonderful remedy. He says:—■
Howick-street, Bathurst, January 30th, 1890.
Dear Sir, —I have lately been taking Clements
Tonic with such wonderful results that I am in-
duced, as a matter of justice, to send a plain
statement of my case, which you are at liberty to
publish in the interest of similar sufferers.
For some years I have suffered acutely
from indigestion, giddiness, languid tired
feelings, no inclination for exertion, hum-
ming noises in the ears—this symptom being
especially troublesome —and extreme lowness of
spirits. I tried many “cures” which did me no
good. I eventually used Clements Tonic, and the
second dose completely cured the noises in my
ears, and during recent attacks they were worse
than ever before, but, thanks to Clements Tonic,
before the first bottle was used my general health
was greatly improved, and I can truthfully state
that it is far superior to any other medicine I ever
took, and I honestly believe that all sufferers will
derive great and permanent benefit from its use.
All unpleasant feelings have entirely left me, and
I shall always use Clements Tonic when out of
sorts. It is a grand pick-me-up, and I am thank-
ful that I gave it a trial. There can be no doubt
even in the most sceptical mind, after such proof
as this.

The unique and phenomenal enterprise of
Messrs. Raphael Tuck and Sons, of London, was
never manifested to such an extent as in their pre-
parations for the Christmas season. It may seem
a commonplace and somewhat coarse idea to try
to indicate the wealth of their artistic novelties
by the ponderosity of the samples of them, but it
may convey some idea of the marvellous profusion
of the new patterns if we say that the specimen-
books alone would nearly fill an ordinary hansom
cab. Those devoted to. the one department of
illustrated books and booklets are quite sufficient
to tax the strength of an ordinary porter to carry
them, while those filled with Christmas and New
Year’s Cards form six huge volumes, embracing
over 1000 sets, and comprising nearly 4000 indivi-
dual designs. This prodigious abundance makes
it extremely difficult to present within the compass
of a notice any adequate idea of the varied and
novel features of even any class into which these
productions might be divided, but of this really
wonderful collection it may be said that for beauty
and excellence, as well as for extent and variety,
it is absolutely unique. Messrs. Tuck have catered
for all tastes, and for all purses, and if their antici-
pations are realised, as we may expect, sales
during the coming season of Christmas and New
Year Cards, will be on an altogether unprecedented
scale.
The display of “Fine Art Furniture” at
Messrs. Wallach’s, Wynyard-square, has always
been admitted one of the finest in Australasia,
but this season they have evidently determined
to eclipse all former efforts. The magnificent
show of dining, drawing and bedroom suites in
every design, both modern and antique, hand-
somely and solidly upholstered, fairly takes one’s
breath. The antique oak sideboards, mounted in
brass, are particularly attractive, and, together
with hall furniture, are a feature worthy of notice,
being both elegant and serviceable. The carpet-
department is represented by all the best-known
manufacturers in England and the Continent,
and is equally remarkable for variety of pattern
and make as for the enormous stock there is to
select from. Oilcloths and linoleums of every
description. A shipment of pianos by all the
well-known makers has just been opened and
added to the already large assortment of pianos,
organs and other musical instruments. Messrs.
Wallaoh, are also showing a most unique assort-
ment of “Xmas Novelties;” among the more
notable are the antique oak clocks, brass-
mounted ; reading and piano lamps in brass and
nickel, copper and bronze ornaments, armour
clocks, bronze statuettes, photo, albums, &c.;
immense variety of Japanese goods, screens,
ornaments. &c. ; flower-stands, rush and willow
tables and chairs in endless profusion. Ladies
about to furnish or refurnish their “boudoirs”
should not fail to pay a visit to this Establishment.
To Messrs. Oolemaneand Sons, Cootamundra. —
Dear Sirs, —I have used the Eucalypte Extract
which I got from you, and am now quite out of it.
Almost every possible complaint I use it for,
including burns, scalds, cuts, bruises, sore throats,
colds, chilblains, neuralgia, toothache, &c., &c.,
and I do not believe there is any disease it will not
cure if persevered with. In ulcerated sore throat
or inflamed throat it is invaluable, having had
personal experience of the fact very recently.
Where children or even adults are badly affected
I usually erect a kind of small tent near a fire-
place, place a kettle on the fire, and when the
water reaches boiling point I pour a quantity of
the Eucalypte Extract into the kettle, and attach
a long tube to the spout of the kettle, and make
the steam play about the patient in the tent. The
steam, owing to the distance it has to travel when
it reaches the patient is thus in a cool form and is
not enervating like hot steam. The whole atmos-
phere in the tent in this manner becomes im-
pregnated with the volatile properties of the Ex-
tract, and the patient is not only relieved but
stimulated. I have by this means “saved tne
lives of one of my children when the medical
attendants said that the only chance of saving the
child’s life would be by performing the operation
of tracheotomy.” In diphtheria I would adopt
similar treatment with the addition of swabbing:
the tonsils and glands frequently with the Extract
on a camel’s hair brush or feather. Nearly every
one here now uses it.—Faithfully yours, J. C.
Ryall, J.P., Editor Cowra Free Press. Cowra,
Aug. 16, ’BB.
The hero of this awkward incident was a
Sydney man by the name of Smyth. Not a
Baron, nor a missionary—just a plain pot-fronted
white shirt and nail-kag Smyth of a pattern as
common in the luncheon-hour as locusts are in
Riverina. And as he sat and scooped up a plat©
of soup with kidney-pie to follow, a stout and
withal shapely maiden, but with a wild, eager look
in both her eyes and a small baby in her arms,
burst in upon the proprietor with an impatient
demand for the whereabouts of “Mr. Smith.”
His glance fell upon the luckless man who was
wiling away the interregnum between soup and
kidneys by rolling up black pills of bread-crumb.
“There’s Mr. Smith, at the end of that table.”
Stepping up with an air of confidence the damsel
deposited the baby on the table beside the empty
soup-plate, merely remarking: “That’s your
baby, Mr. Smith—good-day,” and vanished. With
a wild gurgle he recognised the horror of the
situation, and grabbing the bundle with one hand
rushed out after her. “No you don’t, old man;
it’s your baby right enough, you know, and you
can’t play it back on to me that way.” “Good
God, woman, I tell you it’s not mine. Why,si
spell my name with aY ! ” And it was not until
a constable came up and explained that she must
sue the gentleman in the orthodox fashion that she
reluctantly took the baby back. And then Smyth
“ with a Y ” explained it all to the bobby and to
about 1500 derisive people who were standing by,
and didn’t believe a word of it.

To H. F. Kugblmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. 1 consulted
more than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing mo the slightest
good, but I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About, February last i began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, 1 became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken had one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank pod and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever.
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smytiie, J.P.

great benefit I have derived from your treatment, I should speak, and it is with great pleasure I do so.
I hereby make oath and say that about thirteen (13) years ago I began to feel very unwell, and had
continual and great pain from an internal disease, which my medical attendant completely failed to
benefit or to correctly diagnose. I continued his treatment for more than a year, with great persever-
ance, but instead of improving any I gradually became worse, my doctor first prescribing one drug,
then another in endless succession without relief. I then consulted another eminent medical man,
Supposed to be the most skilled in Melbourne. I continued his treatment for another year, during
which time I took an immense amount of Drugs, Poisons, and Minerals, none of which did me the
least good whatever. I continued to become worse and to lose flesh daily, and the pain I suffered day
and night was most intense. This Doctor then declared my case to be perfectly hopeless, and that it
was only a matter of time and the disease would kill me. In despair I then tried another Doctor of
reputed skill, and he declared that no case of the disease from which I suffered had ever been known
to recover, but that he could ease me, and prescribed chlorodyne, which pOison only stupified me, but
did not ease my complaint in the least. I therefore abandoned this treatment and tried others, all of
which proved to be only blind experiments, as none did me even temporary good. In this state I was
therefore forced to pass nine years of wretched existence ; many times I would have been glad (had it
been God s will) to have died, and so ended my misery, as I never hoped to be well again. Eventually,
however (and I thank God for it), I saw your address in the paper, and I made up my mind to consult
you immediately, which I did, and you at oncp told me the correct nature of my disease, and without
hesitation I accepted your treatment and continued it for a good many months, during which time I
rapidly recovered, and have been completely cured now for over a year, which generally is a good
indication of success. Before I close I would further say I thank God I was ever induced to come to
you, and I pray you may be long spared to do good to suffering humanity, and I hope that through
reading my ease that sufferers with chronic diseases, supposed to be incurable, will consult you, and
discard the old-fashioned Doctors and their destructive drugs and operations. Trusting that this will
do good, I remain, dear sir, yours very sincerely, Mbs. ELIZABETH LEE.
. I; Elizabeth Lee, of Kew, Victoria, do solemnly and sincerely declare the above written statement
signed by me is, to the best of my knowledge and belief, true in every particular, and I make this
declaration conscientiously believing the same to be true, and by virtue of an Act of Parliament
of Victoria rendering persons making a false declaration punishable for wilful and corrupt perjury.
_ . ~ , _ Mbs. ELIZABETH LEE, Cobden-street, Kew.

Bannerman-street, Sandhurst, October 30, 1888.
Mr. Kugelmann, 172-174 William Street, Melbourne.
Dear Sir, —I consider it a duty to suffering humanity to add
my testimony to your undoubted skill and ability in successfully
treating chronic disease. Seven years ago I began to have diffi-
culty in breathing, and my lungs began to be affected, with great
tightness on the chest, and rapidly getting worse I consulted a
; medical man here and continued his treatment for some time,
? but not getting any improvement and getting also much weaker,
I consulted another doctor here, and continued his treatment for
some years. I always had a great deal of hard matter in my
chest that I could not get away by any medicines that I used :
I had night sweats very badly for years and my appetite almost
entirely left me. In spite op ale treatment and drugs which
I took under medical advice, I rapidly lost flesh until I was
reduced to skin and bone. During this treatment I got the
Rheumatic Fever and Dropsy, my legs and body swelled con-
siderably and also MY heart became affected, and remained in
a weak condition, until I consulted you. The greatest wonder
to me is that I survived the fever in the low condition that I was
in, for I could not eat or drink without great effort. I vomited
most of my food during the whole seven years ; and, although
my chest was frightfully choked up with matter, all the treat-
ment I had failed to bring any sputa away. Consequently my
breathing during the whole of this time remained most difficult
in the extreme, so that my life was a complete misery to myself.
One doctor whom I consulted pronounced me to be in a very bad
state of consumption ; and i was told by one doctor, it was
ONE THING ; AND BY ANOTHER IT WAS SOMETHING ELSE. I tried
the Chinese doctor, as I was determined not to leave anything
undone to relieve me, if not to cure me, of my misery. This
Chinese doctor told me there was a large hole in my right lung,
and treated me for it for six weeks ; but all to no good whatever,
as it all ended in a miserable failure. After this I heard of you,
and decided to consult you, which I did; and on examining me,
you stated you could cure me ; and as you described exactly
MY CONDITION, AND THAT, TOO, WITHOUT MY TELLING YOU ANY
symptoms, I began your treatment at once, and, I am happy to
state, I began to improve rapidly. My heart soon getting into a
better condition, and is now quite right; also my lungs im-
proved fast, and the matter all came away; my appetite
improved gradually, and also my strength, so that I can now
breathe quite freely, and my lungs are quite strong again; and
I can do a hard day’s work again, to the great surprise of many
of my friends, who thought I should have been dead long since,
which, I have no doubt, I would have been before now if I had
not had your treatment at the juncture that I did. Now any
person wishing any further information may have it by calling
on me. To all other sufferers i would say :do not waste
YOUR MONEY BY TAKING THE OLD-FASHIONED DOCTORS’ TREAT-
MENT. Mr. Kugelmann’s medicines are purely herbal, and his
treatment perfectly natural, and that is the treatment that I am
happy and grateful to say has saved me from a life of misery and
untimely death. That this may be circulated far and wide
wherever there may be suffering, is the ardent hope of yours,
•ver gratefully, JOHN RUDE.
Witness—T. Moyle, Sandhurst.
Declared before me this 24th of November, 1888, at Sandhurst,
C. Perry, J.P,
P.S.—I forgot to mention that I was treated by you under
an agreement, which you strictly carried out, and I am quite
satisfied that the treatment I received was worth the money I
paid you.
(Copy of Sworn Certificate.)
27 Commercial Road, South Yarra, November 20th, 1888.
_ Mr. H. R. Kugelmann, —Dear Sir, —I was suffering for eight years from extreme nervous debility
and weakness, so much so that I could not sleep ; at last I became so bad and weak that I could
scarcely walk. My food (what little I took) seemed to do me no good, and I had to leave my situation
through it. I had medical treatment, but got no better. I had just given up in despair, when one of
your circulars came to hand, and, as a last resource I placed myself under your treatment for twelve
months. I began to slowly improve. Three years have now elapsed, and Tam happy to say that I
am, and have been well during that time; my nervousness gradually left me, and I have not had any
return of it. What I felt I wanted was rest, which I seldom got. I think it my duty to thank you,
not only for the medicines I had from you, but also for the extreme kindness and attention X received
at your hands; altogether, I shall ever remember you with gratitude. Several of ray friends have
been to you and have received lasting benefit at your hands. Anyone calling upon me, I shall be
happy to speak to.—l am, dear Sir, yours truly, W. J. EBDALE.
Signed at Prahran, in the colony of Victoria, this 29th Day of November, 1888, before me,
Templeton Bennett, J.P

as loss of vigour and power, decay of the nerves, loss of vitality and all kindred ailments. But by the vast number of people who come to me for proper treatment after
they have tried in vain these advertising legally-qualified doctors, and by the way in which they have been robbed by them proves most conclusively that the whole of
them are a most consummate lot of rascals, who merely fleece their victims of whatever cash they are able to extract from them. They dose them with Quinine and
b-on, Strychnine, Phosphorus, Bromide of Potash, Ammonia, and other vile drugs. These may cause a temporary feeling of relief, only to result in greater misery than
hetore. No one whose nervous system is afflicted in any way should have anything to do with these medical sharks, but should at once consult me, and I will tell them
at once what to do.

To Messrs. Colemane and Sons. —Gentlemen, —
Having received much benefit from the use of
your Victory Ointment and Volatile Eucalypte I
can, with confidence, recommend to all who suffer
from skin disease the use of same. During the past
nine years I suffered from a most annoying skin
disease on my face, caused by the bite of a fly,
and though the most eminent doctors in this
colony treated me for the complaint, it gained
ground, and threatened to unfit me for business.
Acting on the advice of a friend, I determined
to try your Victory Ointment and Volatile
Eucalypte; the result has been most successful.
In cases of bronchitis and colds I have used your
Volatile Eucalypte with success, and in a case
which I met in the bush, far away from medical
aid, it saved a life. —I am, Gentlemen, &c. —
D. M. Campbell. Cootamundra, July 1, 1886.
To Colemane and Sons. —I have been directed by
the Committee of the Temora Hospital to inform
you that the parcel of Eucalypte Extract which
you sent me recently has been used with
very satisfactory results. —Yours faithfully, W.
Wiscon, Hon. Sec. Temora Hospital.

Three Farthings a Day.
“ There is in Sydney an Institute where, for less
than three farthings a day, you have the use of
forty thousand books, five hundred newspapers,
a nd one hundred and fifty other periodicals.”
“But can I take a book home with me to read?”
asked the Inquisitive One.
‘ ‘ Yes, certainly, and not only a book, but if
you are a yearly member, you can take magazines
as well and change them three times a week.”
“What sort of books are lent out?” said the
Inquisitive One again.
“ Here is a batch just arrived by the mail from
England, and all those are for loan. Run over
the titles for yourself.”

The Inquisitive One seemed satisfied, but still a
little sceptical.
“ What sort of people join this Institute?” he
asked.
“ All who are fond of reading. They find that
nowhere in the Australian Colonies is there such
an Institute —and, as a pure matter of business,
nowhere can they get so much for their money.”
“ Why, how do you make that out ? ”
“By a glance at our balance-sheet. Here you
are. Over eleven hundred pounds spent in pur-
chasing books and journals, and all a member pays
is one pound a year or under three farthings' a
day. That is, he gets eleven hundred pounds for
one pound. It is simply ridiculous to think such
advantages should be offered for the money, and
that anyone should hesitate.”
“ Do you get any good Magazines ? ”
“ Here is a list of the principal journals from
England and America. Read for yourself. Then
there are others for the Colonies and elsewhere.”
All the Year Round

about is. bd. to os. per week will meet the payments, and past experience proves that a few shillings

Among the numerous new buildings which
have recently been erected in Melbourne, the
warehouses of Messrs. Oraig, Williamson and
'I homas are the finest. The building is a stately
structure of eight stories, built in the classical
style of architecture, on the sites formerly occupied
by the offices of the Tasmanian Steam Navigation
Company, and of Messrs. Suddeley and Cos., on
the east side of Elizabeth-street, about a couple of
chains from Flinders-street. The lower portion of
the building is of polished Harcourt granite,
which gives a highly finished effect, and the upper
stories are constructed of brick-work coated with
cement. The plans show a frontage of 82ft. Gin.
to Elizabeth-street by a depth of 121 ft., and the
building rises to a height of 120 ft. above
the footway. Access to the various floors
is gained by a wide central staircase, and
for the convenience of visitors two fast-
running Waygood elevators, convey them to the
spacious and well-lighted show-rooms. A service
of hoses for the extinction of fire is provided on
each flat, and every labour-saving appliance to
facilitate communication in the shape of electric
bells and speaking-tubes, is brought into requisi-
tion. Messrs. Craig, Williamson and Thomas
were originally an exclusively wholesale ffirm of
drapers and warehousemen,’ but in 1879 they
determined to embark on an entirely novel course,
and throw open their warehouse to the public,
supplying them with drapely, woollens, and cloth-
ing at wholesale prices, which they were enabled
to do by making their terms with retail customers
strictly cash. In this way they act as distributors
for the manufacturers, and so save to the retail
consumer the 30 per cent, profit which is taken by
the middleman. As evidence of the fact that they
have kept faith with the public, the firm were
compelled to enlarge their original premises in
1881; two years later, to purchase the warehouse
at No. 6 Elizabeth-street, and in the following
year to erect two additional stories, adding
furniture and carpets to the other depart
ments. The firm, however, still finding the
additional accommodation inadequate, owing to
increase of trade, and recognising the inconvenience
arising from defective lighting, as well as the
trouble buyers are put to in having to move frgm
one warehouse to the other before completing their
purchases, procured the adjoining allotments, on
which their new warehouses are erected. Asa
short resume of the history of such an enterpris-
ing firm cannot but be of interest to our readers,
it may be stated that although they bear the name
of a triumvirate who have been long and well
known in commercial circles of this city, the
business is now carried out by Mr. Craig and his
eldest son, who has recently been admitted as his-
partner, Mr, Williamson having retired in 1887,
and Mr. Thomas in 1881. Mr. Stephen Lewis is
the firm’s representative in London, and, with an
able staff of buyers, is engaged in purchasing
goods from home and continental manufacturers.
Anything and everything, from the most trifling
article in drapery to a complete suite of furniture
for a mansion, can be obtained at actual whole-
sale prices for net cash.
To Colemane and Sons. Gentlemen, Our
child, some twenty months old, was suffering
from a severe attack of influenza. For three
nights she was feverish and restless, with copious
running at the nose and eyes, attended with hard
coughing. We were induced to try your Eucalypte
Honey, and I am glad to say the effect was won-
derful, for after the first day of using the Honey
the child has fully recovered her health and spirits.
You can make what use you please of this willing
testimony to the value of the Eucalypte Honey,
and we shall always recommend it to our friends.
—Yours, &c., David Davis, Baptist Minister,
Woollahra.
It is said that St. Louis (U.S.) is infested
by a crank who kisses every pretty woman he
meets and bestows on her a card inscribed : —
“ With the compliments of Jack the kisser.
“ Any lady who has been kissed three times by Jack
and retains this card is entitled to membership in the
Grand Army of the Redeemed.
“ Jack’s kiss purifies, but never defiles. His mission is
divine, and his kiss devoid of sensuality.”
“ His Honor, in summing up, pointed out
that the jury must not be influenced by the mere
fact that Mr. O’Connor was the Postmaster-
General. He did not suppose if Mr. O’Connor
was asked that he would make any claim on
account of his position. Mr. O’Connor, who was
sitting at the solicitors’ side of the bar table,
turned round to the Bench and, half rising from
his seat, bowed and remarked, ‘Certainly not,
your Honor.’ (Laughter.)” And yet they say
that all Irishmen are humorists.
Rev. T. J. Curtis, now on a visit to Sydney
from San Francisco, is the clergyman who (aided
by Rev. Dr. Gilchrist) took so active and manly a
part in endeavouring to obtain the reprieve of
those victims of Lynch law, the Mount Rennie
prisoners.
Baron Huddleston is dead. He was a
clever advocate, but a narrow-minded judge. At
the bar he took part in many celebrated cases;
among them, the prosecution of Dr. Palmer, the
Rugely poisoner, the defence of Mrs. Firebrace,
and the defence of Walter Pook for the Eltham
murder.
Advertising as a medium of conveying to
the public the improvements in every branch of
science cannot be too highly extolled. Important
discoveries would remain dormant if it were not
for the all-powerful press. In the present instance
we consider it our duty to acquaint our readers
with the superior properties of Ewing’s Indelible
Marking Ink. It produces a beautiful and irre-
movable jet-black, and is now being used by ail
the leading hospitals, asylums, &c., throughout
the colonies. We have tried it and are convinced
of its efficacy. We are not anxious to single out
any one individual for popularity, but may be
excused in this instance in consequence of the
superior merits of the preparation.
To Messrs. Colemane and Sons, Cootamundra,
N.S. Wales.—Dear Sirs, —I deem it my duty to
give you a testimonial. For some years past I
have suffered much from a very bad leg, which
gave me continual pain, and at times I was com-
pletely laid up. I tried every human remedy,
and several doctors failed to do me any good. I
may add that my leg was a continual running
from the ankle almost to the knee. Seeing your
advertisement in The Bulletin, I determined to
give your remedies a trial, and with the result
that one small pot of the ointment and a bottle of
your Eucalypte Extract has brought a complete
cure. I can now go about my daily occupation.—
Respectfully yours, Philip Spicer Francis, South
Australia.
Colonial Treasurer M‘Millan has just ap-
pointed an Assistant-Superintendent of N.S.W.
Government Stores at £4OO a year. He says he
found from the Superintendent that there was no
one in the department capable of performing the
duties, and after investigation he “ selected a gen-
tleman from two or three applicants (outside the
service) for the position.” How deliciously
ingenuous ! As if there were no one in the whole
of the N.S.W. Civil Service.capable and desirous
of undertaking that position. How, asked Mr.
Traill, did it ever come to pass that Treasurer
M'Millan had but two or three applicants for a
billet of £4OO a year ? Did he advertise the posi-
tion ? No fear. Another apparently discreditable
N.S.W. Civil Service affair of the week is the case
in which an ex-mining man, with whom Post-
master-General O’Connor had been more or less
directly connected in business, suddenly turns up
as a Post-office clerk—appointed at the expense of
juniors in the service.

Extract from Sydney Morning Herald of August 15.
ISSB, referring to the exhibits of the United States Court,
Centennial Internationa] Exhibition, Melbourne, says:—
A little more to the westward the visitor finds a plat*
unn on which are placed a number of Hammoquette
Reclining Chairs, one of the latest things from' that in-
genious class of Americans who devote their lives td the
invention of objects which shall minister to the fastidious
tastes of the invalid, or meet the luxurious demands of
the wealthy. The Hammoquette Chair, constructed mainly
of American ash, coloured to resemble mahogany, is shown
by R B. Jones, 6 Bridge-street, Sydney, and can, and
will, be made to assume a great variety of shapes. It is
difficult to conceive a more valuable addition to a sick
room, library, study or verandah, than the Hammoquette
Chair must prove.”
Parliament House, Melbourne,
Mr. R. B. Jones, 12th June, 1888.
Dear Sir,—ln reply ta your letter of Bth instant, I
hnvc much pleasure in stating that the nine chairs I pur-
chased from you have given me every satisfaction.
Yours truly,
M. H, DAVIES, M.L.A.,
Speaker Legislative Assembly.
